Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
1
Abstract
This report will investigate the ways to create frequency multipliers also called a frequency synthesizers
and estimate which topology that is best ﬁtted for low power consumption. First system analysis of
frequency synthesizers will be done, to determine how diﬀerent blocks in the system aﬀects performance.
Next a behavioural model in the phase domain will be created to help the design of a frequency synthesizer
to determine how the system will response do diﬀerent kind of inputs, to estimate stability, how close
loop response will look and noise suppression performance. Then a closer look into diﬀerent kind of
system topology that can be used to create a frequency multiplier is investigated, and decision is made
to which topology is best ﬁtted for low power. When the found topologies for low power consumptions
has been found, a literature study is made to ﬁnd out ways how to implement each topology for low
power consumption, and afterwords implemented in cadence spectre, and veriﬁed over process variations.
Page 3 of 157
==PAGE:5==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier

2
Introduction
This master project was created with corporation with Knowles electronic. From Knowles it is desired
to investigation how to create low power frequency synthesizers within the speciﬁcation given in table 1.
The purpose of the PLL is to provide frequency multiplication of a high precision incoming clock and
to generate a clock that is to be used to clock parts of a data acquisition system used in a microphones
signal path.

4
System analysis
In the system analysis section diﬀerent types of frequency synthesizers will be looked at and a choice
of type will be made. Then transfer functions will be developed for each block inside the freqyency
synthesizer, to be able to create a behavioral model of the system. A closer look will be made on type
two PLL’s, and what kind of phase detector that is needed to both detect phase and frequency oﬀset
between a reference clock and the created output clock from the PLL. Phase noise, jitter and spurs
model will be made, to help the designer to indicate what performance parameters that will aﬀect these
criteria.
4.1
Types of frequency synthesizer
A brief overlook of diﬀerent frequency synthesizers is made. Frequency synthesizers can be put into two
categories, direct or indirect synthesizing [1–3].
Frequency Synthesizers
Direct
indirect
Digital
Analog
Digital
Analog
Mix of techniques
Figure 1: Block diagram of frequency synthesizer techniques
Direct synthesising, synthesizes without any frequency transforming elements, it can either be done
analogue or digitally.
 Analogue direct synthesizing (DAS) is done by frequency multiplication by mixing two frequencies
and then ﬁltering. The DAS works by mixing two incoming frequencies and bandpass ﬁlter the
output of the mixer to generate the desired output frequency. The DAS has the beneﬁts of fast
switching between frequencies since it doesn’t contain a closed loop and is only limited by the
propagation delay through the circuit and it has the beneﬁt of low phase noise. Disadvantages
however is large power consumption, large size for wide frequency span and since it is mixing to
achieve the desired frequency from the reference frequencies it creates mixing sidebands that needs
to be ﬁltered out [2, p. 14] [1].
Page 6 of 157
==PAGE:8==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Filter 1
Filter 2
Filter 3
Filter 4
Filter 5
Filter 6
Selected input 
frequencies
Band pass filters
Figure 2: Block diagram of direct analog synthesizer, output frequency determent by number of stages
(m and n) and the input frequency (fa and fb)
 Digital direct synthesizing (DDS) it generated by digital signal process where the desired frequency
is created with a phase accumulator, a lookup table and then reconstructed by a digital to analog
converter.
A reference oscillator generate the base frequency of which the DDS generate the
arbitrary output frequency from, the accumulator and the loop up table generate a discrete time
version of the reference clock which frequency is controlled by a frequency controlled register which
deliver the N input seen in ﬁgure 3. The DAC then converts this digital signal into the analog and a
lowpass ﬁlter rejects replicas generated form zero order hold reconstruction. It has the same beneﬁts
as the analogue direct synthesizing with the extra advantages of very ﬁne frequency resolution, the
main disadvantages is that the digital direct synthesizer can’t achieve higher frequency than the
half the speed of the highest frequency available to it to fulﬁll the Nyquist criteria [4, p. 26-31],
further the DDS generates high spurs levels [2, p. 15] [1].
Accumulator 
N
Look up table
Digital to 
analog 
converter
Low pass filter
Figure 3: Block diagram of direct digital synthesizer, output frequency given my the frequency of the ref-
erence clock (fclk), the number of bits in the accumulator (M) and the added number to the accumulator
each clock cycle (N)
Indirect synthesizing is created by two or more frequency sources and a feedback loop. Two main ways
to do this is either by a phase locked loop (PLL) or a delay locked loop (DLL) [2, p. 16-21].
 A DLL consist of a phase detector (PD), a charge pump (CP), a low pass ﬁlter (LP) and a voltage
controlled delay line (VCDL). The basic concept of a DLL is that the reference frequency is delayed
one clock cycle and then compared at the PD to generate an error signal if they are miss aligned,
next the error signal is feed into a CP that will source or sink an output current out of the LP
Page 7 of 157
==PAGE:9==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
to generate the control voltage (and ﬁlter out high frequency component generated by the phase
detector) to a VCDL. A DLL is a ﬁrst order system so it is inherent stable, it has low phase noise
performance since the VCDL do not accumulate jitter since it is reset every clock cycle with the
reference clock (more on jitter later). A DLL do not generate a frequency, however it delay’s the
incoming reference frequency, to make it able to be used as a synthesizer an edge combiner is
needed. This edge combiner can tap out diﬀerent delayed versions of the reference frequency and
combine them. Disadvantages of DLL’s is they consume high power since the PD and CP is driven
at high speed, another disadvantages is that the duty cycle error from the input is feed through
the system and can by the DLL be increased [5].
Phase detector
Charge pump
Low pass filter
Control voltage
Delay
 element
Reference frequency
Feedback 
frequency
Figure 4: Block diagram of a delay locked loop
 A PLL use the same components as the DLL however the VCDL is swap out with a voltage
controlled oscillator (VCO), which in contrasts to the DLL generates a clock. Further more a
divider can be included in the feedback loop which can divide down the output frequency from
the VCO which will be compared with the reference frequency at the PD. Since the VCO’s output
frequency is divided down before comparison, the output of the PLL will be the dividing ratio
multiply with the reference clock. Since the output frequency is divided down the PD and CP
works at lower frequency’s compared to a DLL, which saves power. However a PLL has higher
phase noise since a jitter error will be generated in the VCO and will not be removed by a clean
clock from the reference. Further more a VCO is working ideally as an integrator which means
with the LP the system will be a second order system which can be unstable, so careful design is
needed to be done, to make the system stable.

4.2
Types of Phase locked loops
There are four main Phase locked loop topologies, to chose from, to ﬁnd the type that can fulﬁll the
speciﬁcation in 1:
 Analog PLL (APLL) which has an analog PD.
 Digital PLL (DPLL) which has a digital PD
 An all digital PLL (ADPLL) which is totally created from digital circuitry
 A software PLL (SPLL) which is created in software in for example a DSP.
The SPLL and the ADPLL would not be useful to design the desired PLL, since the SPLL is implemented
in software [6, p. 257-258] and a ADPLL has large power consumption due to most blocks works at the
high frequency, and furthermore is has high jitter compared to a APLL or DPLL [7, p. 516]. However
if low cost and easy implementation was the focus a ADPLL would be preferred [6, p. 205-216]. Last
the choice is between the APLL and the DPLL, where the DPLL is chosen due to the fact the use of a
digital phase detector an easy implementation can be made to create phase frequency detector (PFD),
the APLL would need an acquisition aid to be able to lock to the correct frequency [7, p. 275].
4.3
Mathematical tools for transfer functions
Now that the chosen topology and type of the PLL is chosen a behavioral model is needed to help design
of the system, ﬁrst a brief introduction to the transfer function is looked at to aid the design.
Page 9 of 157
==PAGE:11==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
The transfer function of a system is a powerful tool that can among others validate how the system will
perform vs frequency, stability of the system and the steady state response. It transforms time domain
diﬀerential equations into a function that is in the complex frequency domain, where mathematical
operations are easier. For example if you have a ordinary diﬀerential equation of a system where impulse
response is known (the transfer function), to know how this system behaves to a speciﬁc input, the
impulse response of the system needs to be convolved with the input signal of interest:
(f ∗g)(t) =
Z ∞
−∞
f(τ)g(t −τ)dτ
(1)
The mathematical operation of convolution as shown in 1 can be very comprehensive, however by deﬁ-
nition convolution in the time domain is the same as multiplication in the frequency domain [4], which
makes manipulations of the system much easier.
To be able to create a transfer function of a system in scope is require to be an linear and time-invariant
(LTI) system with a single input and output where the initial conditions is set to zero. If this is fulﬁlled
a transfer model can be created and the beneﬁt of several transfer models can be cascaded into one single
transfer model that describes the whole system, like in the case of the PLL
The mathematical tools of Laplace transform and z-transform are useful tools to create transfer function
of a system. The Laplace transform analyses continuous-time signals where the z-transform is used for
discrete-time signals [4]. A frequency synthesiser is a discrete system since the input of the reference
signal and the output from the vco is sampled at the phase detector, however the system can be evaluated
as a continuous time system so Laplace can be used if the bandwidth is held lower than the input reference
frequency (more on this later) if the bandwidth of the system gets to high it can no longer be considered
as a continious time system but a sampled system then only the z-transform can be used, however the
criteria for analysing the system in Laplace domain will be fulﬁlled in this project so the z-transform
analyses will not be touch further upon.
The two sided Laplace transform or also called bilateral Laplace transform1 is closely related to the
continuous time Fourier transform (CTFT) that is deﬁned as:
X(jω) = F(x(t)) =
Z ∞
−∞
x(t)e−jωtdt
(2)
And bilateral Laplace transform is deﬁned as:
X(s) = L(x(t)) =
Z ∞
−∞
x(t)e−stdt
(3)
Looking at equation 2 for the CTFT and equation 3 for the bilateral Laplace transform one can note
that the diﬀerent is the time domain function x(t) is multiplied by either a exponential raised to e−jωt
2 or e−st 3. The parameter s in 3 is a complex value that is deﬁned as s = σ + jω where σ is the real
part of s and ω is the complex part. If we expand the s parameter in equation 3 we get:
X(s) = L(x(t)) =
Z ∞
−∞
x(t)e−(σ+jω)tdt =
Z ∞
−∞
(x(t)e−σt)e−jωtdt
(4)
Note that the bilateral laplace transform in 4 looks like the CTFT in equation 2 just with the modiﬁcation
that the signal of interest (this case x(t)) is multiplied by a real exponential e−σt before the Fourier
transform is taken. So if the real part of s is set to zero then the CTFT comes out. This is only true
if the line of σ = 0 is within the Region of Convergence, since you can’t take the Fourier transform of
an unstable system, however the Laplace transform can. In other words when σ = 0 in equation 4, the
transform must obey the Dirichlet’s theorem for the CTFT [4].
The the Laplace transform can take the transform of a system that is not stable as mention above
(doesn’t obey the Dirichlet’s theorem) since it is multiplying the CTFT with an exponential decaying
1called bilateral since it goes from −∞to ∞in respect to the unilateral Laplace transform which goes from 0 to ∞
Page 10 of 157
==PAGE:12==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
function e−σ, which will force it to converge. Since it allowed to use the exponential function is as for
the Fourier transform that decomposes the signal into a sum of sinusoidal, the integral of derivitive of
an exponential function is an exponential function, like the derivative or integral of a sinusoidal is a
sinusoidal, which both obey the rule of a LTI system [8].
4.3.1
The s-plane
As described in section 4.3 the Laplace transform decomposes a signal into its exponential and sinusoidal
components, and this can be plotted in the s-plane. The s-plane is a three dimensional plane in corre-
spond to the Fourier transforms two dimensional plane (amplitude and frequency). The s-plane contain
amplitude and frequency as the Fourier on the imaginary axis, and on the real axis the exponential
component. Figure 6 shows a s-plane plot with the poles corresponding homogeneous response created
in matlab:
y(t) =
n
X
i=1
Ciepit
(5)
From the homogeneous response seen in equation 5 a real pole in the left hand side of the s-plane pi = −σ
will create an exponential decaying function Ce−σt = e−0.1t (see ﬁgure 6 left bottom plot), where the
value of C correspond to the initial conditions (for all plots in ﬁgure 6) the initial condition is 1, the
value of the numerator of the transfer function).
A complex conjugated pole in the left hand side of the s-plane −σ±jω will create a exponential decaying
sinusoidal Ae−sigmasin(ωt + φ) = e−0.1tsin(1t) where A=C and φ = 0 (see ﬁgure 6 top left plot). These
two response as describe a stable system, which means that the system will fall down to a steady state
output.
A complex conjugated pole on the imaginary axis will create an oscillating response which is either
decaying or rising Asin(ωt + φ) = sin(1t) , this is called marginal stable.
A pole at origin will create a response with constant amplitude set by the initial conditions.
The same eﬀect will happen for a real and complex conjugated pole in the left half plane but with
the change that the homogeneous response will be exponential growing since eσ is raised to a positive
number. In this region the system is said to be unstable, since it will exponential grow (in real life system
this is not possible, some physical parameters will limit the circuits output) [8].

Now that the transform function is introduce, is it needed to generate transfer function of the phase

Where the steady state error of the system will can be validated in respect to type of PLL (note when
talking about type it means how many integrates there is in the PLL not if it is a analog, digital or
software PLL). One shall note that all transfer functions of the diﬀerent system blocks is without the
present of noise.

4.4.1
Phase detector and phase frequency detector
A phase detector can be compared with the function of a mixer which multiplies the two incoming signals.
To ﬁnd the output of a mixer we take two signals and multiply them:
Vref(t) = V pref · cos(ωref · t + Θe)
(7)
Where Vref(t) is the reference signal, ωref is the angular frequency of the reference signal and Θe is the
error between the phases of the reference signal and the vco signal:
Vvco(t) = V pvco · cos(ωvco · t)
(8)
The product of equation 7 and 8 gives:
Vout = (V pref · cos(ωref · t + Θe)) · (V pvco · cos(ωvco · t))
(9)
Using the product to sum trigonometric identity (2 · cos(Θ) · cos(Φ) = cos(Θ −Φ) + cos(Θ + Φ)) on
equation 9 one get:
2 · Vout = Vref(t) · Vvco(t) · [cos(ωref · t + Θe −ωvco · t) + cos(ωref · t + Θe + ωvco · t)]
(10)
Taking away the high frequency component in equation 10 (cos(ωref · t + Θe + ωvco · t)) one is left with:
2·Voutlow = Vref(t)·Vvco(t)·[cos(ωref·t+Θe−ωvco·t)] = 0.5·Vref(t)·Vvco(t)·[cos(ωref·t+Θe−ωvco·t)] (11)
Collecting terms from equation 11 one get:
Voutlow = Vmix · cos(ωdiff · t + Θe)
(12)
Page 13 of 157
==PAGE:15==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Where Vmix = 0.5 · Vref(t) · Vvco(t) which is the output voltage of the mixer and ωdiff = ωref −ωvco is
the frequency diﬀerence between the reference signal and the returning vco frequency. If equation 12 is
diﬀerentiated one get the the gradually change in phase sloop of the mixer which gives:
Voutps = dVoutlow
dΘe
= Vmix · sin(Θe)
(13)
Where Voutps is the mixer’s phase slope. One shall note that in equation 13 the frequency diﬀerence
ωdiff is set to zero before diﬀerentiating.
From equation 13 it is seen that the phase slope of the mixer has its highest value when the phase error
Θe has a value of π
2 or equal to 90◦phase shift between input signals, which will lead to the value of the
mixing product of the two input signals voltage level Vmix, and the output of the mixer has its minimum
phase slope when Θe equals zero. This can be collected to the term:
Vd = Kd · (Θref −Θvco) = Kd · (Θe)
(14)
Where Vd is equal to Voutps and Kd is the phase detector gain, normal given in volts per radians ( V
rad)
or amperes per radian ( I
rad).
The derivation of equation 7 to 14 is is deduced from [7,9].
4.4.2
Charge pump and Opamp
A charge pump or an Opamp can be used to take the incoming signal from the PD and realize an
integrator with the loop ﬁlter, or both if the CP delivers it current pulses to an active ﬁlter (loop ﬁlter
wrap around the Opamp). The CP converts the output from the phase detector into a charging or
discharging current that will source or sink current out from the capacitor in the loop ﬁlter, the gain of
the charge pump is the size of the source and sink current respectively: Icp. For an Opamp it converts
the output from the phase detector into a voltage source that has either positive or negative voltage
polarity that will charge and discharges the capacitor VOpamp .

4.4.4
Divider
The divider is used if multiplication of the reference frequency is desired. It will divide down the VCO
frequency by the division ratio, before it is feed back to the PD, to be compared to the reference frequency.
By this operation the reference frequency is multiplied by the division ratio. The divider is model by
the transfer function of
1
N where N is the division ratio [2, p. 34]. A note is that this transfer function
doesn’t take care of the delay the divider creates, which will create a small degradation of the phase
margin by the time shift it introduces given by e
s·Td
N , where N is the divison ratio and Td is the delay
in feedback-loop which comes from the divider, however this is normally not model, since it is in most
cases only a few degrees loss in phase margin.
4.4.5
Loop ﬁlter type and order
The loop ﬁlter can take many transfer function all depending of the order. The loop ﬁlter will determine
what order and type the PLL will take, where the order is the amount of roots in the transfer function
denominator, and type comes down to how many poles there is located at origin of the s plane, which
also indicates how many integrators there is in the system (note that the type also comes down from
that the phase detector with the charge pump or opamp creates the extra integrator in the system).
The most common type of PLL is the type two second order PLL. It gives most freedom to the designer
in regards of controlling system characteristic versus complexity (higher orders and types is harder to
design, due to stability issues, meaning more care is needed to place poles and zeros so the system is
stable).
The loop ﬁlter can be created either passive or as active. The passive loop ﬁlter provided better noise
performance since it only contributes with is thermal noise component where an active ﬁlter would also
introduce ﬂicker noise from the transistor usage. However a active ﬁlter can be use if the charge pump
isn’t able to create the desired control voltage level, then the active ﬁlter can provide gain or a level shift.
A brief overlook of diﬀerent types and orders of PLL is looked at to help explain what the steady state
error for the diﬀerent type will be. Note that the divider path is set to one, this is why it isn’t seen in
any of the following equations.
 Type one, ﬁrst order PLL.
The type one PLL only contains one integrator and that is from the VCO. If a type one PLL is of
ﬁrst order, then no ﬁlter is used, this does that the only parameter the designer has control over
is the loop gain. For a type one, ﬁrst order PLL to achieve good tracking of error high DC gain is
needed (see equation 22), however the bandwidth will follow since the 3dB bandwidth of the PLL
is equal to the DC gain fort a type one ﬁrst order. This make it impossible to create a narrow
band PLL with good tracking performance, due to this ﬁrst order type one PLL is not seen that
often [13, p. 22].
Kdc = Kd · KO · K1 = K
(19)
G(s) = K

Equation 19 to 22 indicates the open loop gain, the open loop transfer function, closed loop transfer
function and error transfer function for the type one ﬁrst order pll respectively [13, p. 22]. Where
in equation 19 Kd is the gain of the phase detector, KO is the gain of the VCO and K1 is the gain
of the proportional path through the loop ﬁlter. The book [13] splits the loop ﬁlter into two path,
a proportional and integral part coming from control theory.
 Type one, second order PLL with lag ﬁlter.
The second order type 1 PLL has the same DC gain as the ﬁrst order type one PLL, it has the
transfer function and error function as given [13, p. 23]:

Now two parameters is available for design due to the increase of order, the loop gain and the time
constant of the ﬁlter. For this particular design with lag ﬁlter large damping is needed for large DC
gain, which reduces the bandwidth (see equation 26). The second order type one PLL will have a
40dB/decade roll of due to the order of the polynomial and has no gain peaking for damping factors
above 0.707, which has made this type of PLL popular for telecommunication repeaters [13, p. 23].
 Type two PLL.
The type two PLL has two integrators in the system, one from the VCO and one created by the
ﬁlter with either a CP or Opamp. The double pole at origin give the PLL a dc gain of KDC = ∞,
this does trade oﬀbetween DC gain and bandwidth can’t be control in the same way as the type 1
PLL [13, p. 24]. A type two PLL will always need a zero in the numerator of the polynomial if the
system shall be stable. It comes from that type two system will have a roll oﬀ40dB/decade from
origin, the phase starts at −180◦and stays there when looking at the open loop transfer function.
So the 0dB cross over will always happen at a −180◦phase, which sets the system on the stability
boundary, any small disturbance can push the system into oscillation (the roots for a type two
system only containing the integrating part has complex pole pair). The same fenomernen can
be seen if looking at the closed loop transfer function, here the phase will start at 0◦and rapidly
change to −180◦at the location of the complex poles, and the gain will peak. The gain peaking
can be seen as a metastable point where only small disturbance needs to change the state of the
system. So a rule is that there should always be Type −1 zeros in the transfer function to be able
to make the system stable. So for a type 2 system at least one zero is needed.
G(s) = Kd · KO · K2

 Type two, second order PLL.
The type two, second order PLL is the most common ﬁlter used. Often an extra capacitance which
will ad a pole is added to ﬁlter out high frequency components from the phase detector, which in
principle makes it a third order ﬁlter, however it is normal design the pole to be located high in

 Type two, higher orders than second.
Higher orders than second for type two PLL isn’t often seen, since they are hard to make stable due
to the increase loop complexity, and in most cases a second order will be suﬃcient for the design.
However if the design needs a higher roll oﬀat the cut oﬀfrequency due to for example ﬁltering
out high frequency component from the phase detector, the third order or higher, type two pll can
be used [13, p. 28].
4.5
Closer look at type two, second order PLL
It is decided to use a type two second order charge pump PLL (CPPLL) is decided to use, since it gives
ﬂexible control of the DPPL, it is able to create steady state errors of zero for step and ramp inputs (as
will be seen in section 4.5.4) which is needed for this design, since the input reference can change. A
third order ﬁlter is not chosen since it is assumed that the incoming reference clock noise isn’t needed
to suppress more of the 40dB per decade the second order loop will achieve. The block diagram of the
CPPLL with a lead lag ﬁlter is shown in ﬁgure 8.

4.5.1
Response peaking
The damping factor ζ seen in second order loops is an important parameters that controls gain peaking
(if the system is going to have an overshoot). The system would not have any peaking in the response
for values of ζ higher than 0.707. However a second order type 2 PLL will always have some peaking
due to the zero placed in the numerator, which will rise the response of the system even for very large
values of ζ [13, p. 18]. From the transfer function given in equation 34 the gain peaking can be found
from [13, p. 19]:
Gpeak = 10 · log10

the damping factor
If the system didn’t have the zero, the critical damping would be a value of ζ = 1, where no overshoot
would occur at all, due to the poles would be real. Damping less than 1 the poles will be complex and
conjugated, and smaller values of ζ will move the complex conjucated poles closer to the imaginary axis
and will create even larger overshoot (see picture 6).

ωc =
s
K2 + K ·
p
K2 + 4 · ω2z
2
(42)
There is a limit to the unity bandwidth a PLL can have. This is due to that the PLL is a sample
system coming from that a phase frequency detector only samples on the rising or falling edge of the
input reference. Only on this sampling the PFD can correct the phase error, so a minimum ratio between
bandwidth to reference input frequency has to be maintain to keep the system stable, and act like a linear
time invariant system (which also is mandatory for the Laplace transform to be used).A explanation to
the limited bandwidth of the DPLL, can be explained by the example of applying a step input to
the DPPL. When the step input is applied the DPLL acts by increasing the output frequency, if the
sampling interval is large so the output frequency grows beyond the step impuls size, an overcorrection
has occurred. Now the DPLL at next sample interval will correct the output frequency in the opposite
direction and again a overcorrection will occur in the opposite direction, so oscillation has started.
Many books states that the bandwidth is limited to ten times lower than the reference frequency [2,12,
14, 15] but do not give an detailed explanation where this number comes from, expect the explanation
that a DPLL is a sample system. The journal article [16] a more detailed look into the boundary of the
ratio of PLL bandwidth versus reference clock, where [17] take this researchers a step further. It is found
from [17] that the bandwidth limit for a type two second order charge pump PLL can be found by:
KP LL
ωz
> 1, ζ > 0.5

: ωref
KP LL
> π
(43)
KP LL
ωz
< 1, ζ < 0.5

: ωref
ωz
> π
(44)
From equation 44 for damping factors higher than 0.5 shows that the reference frequency must be larger
than KP LL · π and for lower damping factors of 0.5 equation 43 states that the reference frequency must
be larger than ωz ·π. Since a higher ratio is chosen in many cases is to give margin in the design to make
sure the bandwidth is always less than π · KP LL (for damping higher than 0.5).

4.6
Acquisition of lock
Acquisition of lock describes the process of the PLL to get into locked state from an unlock one. It
is determine by the system parameters but especially the kind of phase detector that is used.
The
acquisition can be split into four groups [6, p. 50]:
 Hold range (∆ωH). This is the range of which the PLL is able to maintain locked conditions and
be stable.
 Pull-in range (∆ωP ). This is the range where the PLL always becomes locked but can be a slow
process.
Page 28 of 157
==PAGE:30==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
 Pull-out range (∆ωP O. This is the range of which the frequency step at the input of the PLL was
large and made the PLL looses phase tracking. In this range the PLL normally will acquire lock
again.
 Lock range (∆ωL). Locked range is the area where the PLL will acquire lock again if lost within
one beat note (beat note comes from a analog multiplier phase detector, the the beat note is the
frequency diﬀerence between the reference and VCO frequency after multiplied).
ω0
±ΔωL 
±ΔωPO 
±ΔωP 
±ΔωH 
ω
Figure 23: Illustration of the diﬀerent lock ranges. ω on the x-axis is the reference frequency and ω0 is
the oscillating frequency of the VCO (the desired frequency in locked condition).
As mention above the acquisition of lock is determine by the system parameters and the type of phase
detector. Only the phase frequency detector will be going into detail since it is the topology that will
be used. However a brief mention to other kinds of phase detector will be mention for the pull in range,
since this also indicates why they need an frequency acquisition aid to become locked.
4.6.1
Hold range
The hold range can be found by applying a ramp input to the error transfer function and applying the
ﬁnal value theorem as shown in section 4.5.4. To normalize to any given type or order PLL the transfer

4.6.2
Pull-in range
The pull-in range is a nonlinear process only an approximation can be achieved. The book [6, p. 341-351]
solves for the solution for diﬀerent kinds of phase detectors and loop ﬁlters, where the book [7, p. 112-
123] solves the second order nonlinear ordinary diﬀerential equation (the nonlinear ordinary diﬀerntial
equation arises from a PLL is a sample system due to the phase detector) in the time domain, but doesn’t
give an exact solution to the pull-in range as [6] does. Only the results of the work from [6] will be cited
here however an explanation of the process is given:
When the system is initial turned on, it is assumed that the reference frequency and the VCO frequency
is apart (further apart than the lick range), which makes the system in an unlock state. The acquisition
of lock in this state is called the pull-in range, where the acquisition of the lock can be split into self-
acquisition and aided-acquisition. The self-acquisition the system will try go into a locked state and is
able to, however can lock on to the wrong frequency, that is where the aided system is comes in and
help the system to acquired lock. From [6] the pull-in range of a PFD is found to be ∞, this is due to
that the PFD with the loop ﬁlter acts like an ideal integrator with inﬁnite gain at DC. It acts like an
integrator since the PFD has a tristate output when the PFD is in reset. During this reset period the
loop capacitors charge is keept constant (if leakage is neglected), which acts like an integration [6, p. 67].
In table 3 the pull in range for low and high loop gain is summarized for an analog multiplier, EXOR,
JK-ﬂipﬂop and PFD phase detector [6, p. 74-76].

4.6.3
Pull-out range
The pull-out range is the frequency step at the input of the PFD that will create a phase error larger than
2π which will make the PFD loose track. For the PFD is operates linear over the range −2π < Θe < 2π
which does that the pull-out range can be calculated by a linear model [6, p. 72].
This is done by
applying small frequency steps to the reference input and calculating the phase error. This is shown to
be a damped oscillation that is dependent on the natural frequency and the damping factor of the PLL.
Three diﬀerent result is found for damping for a damping factor less, equal and larger than one. But to
easy calculations a least square ﬁt is made and the pull-out range is found to be [6, p. 73]:
∆ωP O = 11.55 · ωn · (ζ + 0.5)
(63)
4.6.4
Lock range
The deﬁnition and concept of lock range can be illustrated by ﬁgure 24. The top graph in ﬁgure 24
shows the PFD’s average output, if the change on the reference is smaller than this, the PLL is able to
lock within one beat note. An example of this is shown in the middle and lower graph in 24. ω0−sc is the
downscaled average VCO frequency seen at the input of the PFD, where ω1 is the reference signal and
ω2 denotes the amplitude of the frequency deviation for the downscaled VCO frequency. In the middle
graph in ﬁgure 24 the peak diﬀerence between the reference clock ω1 and the downscaled VCO clock ω2
denoted by ∆ω is larger than the range of which the PFD can detect (over 2π), therefore lock will not
occur in one beat note. However in the lower graph of ﬁgure 24 the diﬀerence between ω1 and ω2 is
smaller than the range of which the PFD can detect and it will lock in one beat note.

4.7
Phase noise
Phase noise is a measurement of how good the spectral purity of the frequency synthesizer is. The phase
noise can be split into two categories, device noise like thermal or ﬂicker noise which is a random process
and spurious signals (more on this in section 4.9.) that is a deterministic process. The phase noise
can be measured in many diﬀerent ways, where the most common is the single side band (SSB) phase
noise which is the ratio of noise power in 1Hz bandwidth at a oﬀset from the signal power (the carrier)
and has the units of dBc
Hz . Sometimes the phase noise is speciﬁed as the double side band (DSB) phase
noise which is equal to twice the size of the SSB phase noise, since it is assumed that the phase noise is
symmetrically spread around the carrier. The SSB phase noise is deﬁned as:
Lfm = 10 log
Pnoise(fm)
Psignal

(73)
Where the noise Pnoise(fm) is the noise at the speciﬁc frequency oﬀset from the carrier fm = f −fc and
Psignal is the power of the carrier. The noise in the PLL can also be split into the contribution from
amplitude and phase modulation, where the SSB phase noise can be written as:
Lfm = 10 log
SΦ(fm)
2
+ SA(fm)
2

(74)
Where SΦ(fm) is the DSB phase noise spectral density and S(fm) is the DSB amplitude noise spectral
density. Amplitude phase noise is normal not model since it is primarily phase noise that dominates in
an oscillator, since oscillators accumulates phase ﬂuctuations [13, p. 144].
Page 33 of 157
==PAGE:35==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
4.7.1
PLL noise model
It is desired to crease a phase noise model of the PLL that can indicate what noise sources dominates
over the bandwidth of the PLL and after, to help indicate what blocks and parameters there increases
and decrease the noise. Further it is desired to implement this into the phase model to evaluate the
total PLL noise performance. Since this will be done in the phase domain with a model is that it is
possible to get the phase noise out of each individual block by creating a periodic steady state (PSS)
simulation in cadence spectre, with a PSS noise simulation afterwords, but it is however not possible to
measure the total system phase noise on top level (all blocks connected). This is due to that the PSS
cannot operate on a system that both has a driven input (the created reference frequency by a voltage
source) and an autonomous (the VCO), however harmonic balance (HB) simulation, can do this by the
option of semi-autonomous, however it is highly recommended to use PSS with shooting harmonics op-
tion for ring oscillators [18] to get correct results (the choice of ring oscillator is explained in section 6.1.5).
The block diagram shown in ﬁgure 25 is used to determine the noise transfer function to determine how
the noise of each individual block will eﬀect the output noise of the CPPLL.

by :

G(s)

Where G(s) is deﬁned as the open loop transfer function of as seen in equation 33, and with values for
a CPPLL:
G(s) = KP D · KCP · F(s) · KV CO · KDIV
s
= Icp · KV CO · F(s)
2 · π · N · s
(81)
And the total rms output noise can be found as:
θ2
out = (θref · Nref)2 + (θP F D · Npfd)2 + (icp · Ncp)2 + (θvco · Nvco)2 + (θdiv · Ndiv)2
(82)
From the found noise transfer functions how each individual block it can be seen that the reference,
PFD, CP and divider each has a lowpass response, where the loop ﬁlter noise has a band-pass response
and the VCO noise has a high-pass response. From this it can be seen that the within the bandwidth
of the CPPLL it will be the reference, PFD and CP noise that is dominating, where it is the reference
noise that will set the noise ﬂoor, and the PFD and CP will add noise on top of this. After the loop
bandwidth it will be mainly the VCO noise that is seen. One shall note that both the reference, PFD,
CP and divider noise each is increase by the division ratio inside the loop bandwidth.
So if the reference oscillator has poor phase noise performance it is desired to decrease the bandwidth of
the CPPLL, so the -20dB/decade roll oﬀhappens as soon as possible to suppress the reference oscillator
noise. However if is the VCO inside the CPPLL that will be the dominating noise factor it is desired
to increase the bandwidth as much as possible to suppress the noise from the VCO. To reduce the noise
inside the bandwidth of the CPPLL, it can be seen from both equation 76, 77 and 79 increase the gain
of the PFD helps suppress the PFD and CP noise (the noise from the VCO is suppress in equation 79,
due to the increase size of equation 81 with increase of KP D). Further to reduce the noise contribution
from the CP the gain of the CP should be increased (large output current, while keeping noise level the
same). Also to reduce the noise within the bandwidth the division ratio should be kept as low as possible
since as explained above increases the noise within the bandwidth by its division factor.
If it is the noise from the VCO after the bandwidth that is dominating and needs to be reduces, one
shall reduce the VCO gain as much as possible and of the loop ﬁlter. Also for both the noise within the
bandwidth and after, the noise of each individual block can be reduce if noise is a problem in the desired
region.
4.7.2
Noise bandwidth
If the PLL is used to clean up a noisy input signal a term that is often used to determine how well this
will be done by the PLL is noise bandwidth. It assumes that the input noise is white and stationary. If
this assumption is used the noise bandwidth of a type two second order PLL is deﬁne as [6, p. 83-87]:
BL = ωn

the PLL is able to improve the SNR by a ratio of

4.7.4
Mathematical noise model of oscillator
To ﬁnd out what parameters there should be tuned for to lower the phase noise in the oscillator is the
famous mathematical model from Leeson’s given as [21, p. 26]:
L(fm) = 10 · log
 
2 · F · k · T
Ps
·

f0
2 · QL · fm
2
·

1 + fl
fm
!
(85)
Where in equation 85, QL is the loaded quality factor of resonator, f0 is the oscillation frequency, Ps is
the power of the signal in the oscillator, fm is the frequency oﬀset from the center frequency, F is the
noise factor of the active device, k is Boltzmann’s constant, T is the temperature in Kelvin and fl is the
ﬂicker corner frequency. Equation 85 gives an intuitive idea what to design for to reduce the oscillator
noise. To reduce the noise low noise factor F is desired and increase quality factor QL and power Ps.
Leesons phase noise model will only give an intuitive evaluation of the phase noise, since it is based on the
assumption that the oscillator works like a linear time-invariant system, which is not true for a CPPLL
(it is a sample system due to the PFD), further Leesons model assumes that only additive white noise
is present which is not true in a CMOS implemented oscillator that has ﬂicker noise [13, p. 215]. More
mathematically heavy solution has been presented by Hajimiri [22] and Demir [23] that uses a nonlinear
analysis of the oscillator, however they do not give the designer an intuitive idea what parameters
there should be design for to reduce noise, however they will give a more precise phase noise number
out [24, p. 31].
4.8
Jitter
Like phase noise determine the purity of the signal spectral, jitter can be said being to measure the
purity of the time domain of the signal. Jitter is a variable that determine time ﬂuctuations of the
signal from an ideal position. A 5Mhz clock signal would ideally have a period of 20 micro seconds with
alternating edges every 10 micro seconds, however in real implementation the period has variations to it
ideal locations which is refereed to as jitter. Jitter can be determine in diﬀerence ways, where absolute
jitter is a measurement of the absolute diﬀerence for a clock edge from is ideal position denoted by
Page 37 of 157
==PAGE:39==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
(tn −n · T) where T is the periode of the nominal frequency and tn is the n’th period. The absolute
jitter can be found from the total area of power spectrum if the process of the phase noise is a stationary
process by [25]:
σ2
A =
1
(2 · π · f0)2
Z
f0
2
−f0
2
Sθ(f)df
(86)
Where in equation 86 f0 is the nominal frequency, and Sθ(f) is the total area of the power spectrum.
Jitter can also be speciﬁed as period jitter, which indicates the variation in the period of the clock from
is nominal position speciﬁed as (jn = tn+1 −tn −T) which also can be speciﬁed as k-cycle jitter by
(jn(kT) = tn+k −tn −kT) where k speciﬁed k cycles the jitter is wish to be calculated over, also refereed
to as long term jitter. The period jitter can be found like the absolute jitter by integrating the phase
noise power spectrum if assumed being stationary [25]:
σ2

Last jitter can also be speciﬁed as cycle to cycle jitter or adjacent period jitter speciﬁed as ((∆jn) =
jn+1 −jn = (tn+2 −tn+1)−(tn+1 −tn)), which indicates the displacement between two consecutive clock
periods. It can also be determine over a period of k cycles by (∆jn(kT) = tn+2k −2tn+k + tn). Also the
cycle to cycle jitter can be found from the phase noise power spectrum again assuming it is a stationary
process by [25]:
σ2

Cycle to cycle jitter is the only jitter metric that can be used if ﬂicker noise is present in the system, since
absolute and periode jitter is are unbound in the present of ﬂicker noise. This can be explained as long
term jitter in a PLL is strongly dependent on the dynamics of the PLL, where cycle to cycle jitter is much
less [26, p. 25] [27]. One shall note that equation 86 to 88 is obtain if the power spectrum is considered
stationary, however present of ﬂicker noise which isn’t a stationary process, makes the assumption of
integrating the power spectrum not valid, if the power spectrum is dominated by ﬂicker noise. Flicker
noise has long term correlation and inﬁnite variance since the variance increases as a function of the
measured sequence length [25]. The paper by Lee [25] creates a model that relates absolute jitter for a
type two, second order PLL in respect to natural frequency and damping factor, that takes ﬂicker noise
into account:
σ2

It can be found from equation 89 that to minimize the absolute jitter the natural frequency shall be
increased and a reduction of VCO jitter. Since no other sources in the PLL is meantion like the PFD,
CP, Loop ﬁlter or divider is that it is assumed that the dominate block to contributes to jitter is from
the VCO itself and or the reference clock.
From Lee the optimization process to either reduce the
ﬂicker noise or white noise is dertermint by which noise contribution is dominant over the measurement
interval. If ﬂicker noise and white noise is equally contributes to the absolute jitter, it is the white noise
that dominates for short term periods, wit measurement interval less than
1
ωn . If the ﬂicker noise is a
factor of 40 higher than the white noise then white noise is dominant in the interval shorter than 0.01
ωn ,
where for longer measurement intervals the ﬂicker noise is dominant.
4.9
Spurs
Spurs is non desired frequency content that isn’t related to the VCO and its harmonics that can be
seen as sidebands in the frequency spectrum of the PLL. The largest spurs is created from the reference
clock [7, p. 131], which is denoted as reference spurs. The are mainly created by current mismatch in
the CP, PD delay create to get rid of deadzone plus the CP delay for it to turn on, leakage in the CP
and loop ﬁlter and charge injection in the CP.
4.9.1
Spurs generated by charge pump and PFD due to current mismatch
For a CP it is desired to reduce the current mismatch as much as possible, this is because mismatch
between the source and sink current in the idle state where the net current charging in and out of the
loop ﬁlter can cause a phase oﬀset, which will increase the level of reference spurs. In idle state the CP
will source and sink current at the same time where their net current to the loop ﬁlter shall be zero. The
reason why the CP is on in idle state is to get rid of dead zone. Dead zone is a term which comes from
when the PFD with the CP isn’t able to react to very small phase error between the reference and VCO
frequency, since the PFD can’t generate small pulses and the CP can’t turn on/oﬀfrom small pulses,
which would create a dead zone in the closed loop, which greatly reduces the gain of the system. To
prevent dead zone a delay is added to the PFD to create minimum width pulses. The power level of the
reference spurs this creates in the loop for a CPPLL with second order passive loop ﬁlter can be found
from [28]:
Θecp = 2 · π · ∆ton
Tref
·
Icp + ∆I
Icp
−1

(94)
Equation 94 calculates the phase oﬀset the current mismatch between the charge pump current Icp. ∆ton
is the turn on time of the PFD, Tref is the time periode of the reference clock and ∆I is the current
mismatch of the CP. The value of equation 94 can be inserted into equation 95 and the power of the
reference spurs can be found in dBc:
Pr = 20 log
 √
2 · Icp·R
2·π · Θecp · Kvco
2 · fref
!
−20 · log
fref
fp1

(95)
Where in equation 95 R is the resistor value in the loop ﬁlter, Kvco is the gain of the VCO, fref is
the reference frequency and fp1 is the frequency of the pole from the loop ﬁlter. It can be seen from
equation 95 that to reduce the spurs levels the bandwidth of the CPPLL should be reduce this can be
seen if equation 106 is substituted into equation 95 and it is assumed that the DC gain is equal to the
unity bandwidth which is the case for high damping factors. It can also be seen from equation 94 that
increasing the charge pump current while maintaining same current mismatch would reduce the phase
oﬀset and the turn on time of the PFD should be minimized, this will also reduce the spur level.
Page 39 of 157
==PAGE:41==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
4.9.2
Spurs generated from leakage current
Leakage in the loop ﬁlter or output of the CP, also generates reference spurs, that will be seen in the
phase spectrum of the CPPLL. Equation 95 can be used to calculate the power level of reference spurs
generated by leakage, where Θecp is change out with the following:
Θeleak = 2 · π · Ileak
Icp
(96)
Where Ileak in equation 96 is the leakage current size. From equation 96 it can be seen that leakage
current can generate high phase oﬀset if the ratio between the CP current and the leakage current is
small. So for small CP current, it sets a constrain on the loop ﬁlter, where the capacitance created in
it should be avoided to be made out of mos capacitors, due to their high leakage current compared to a
metal insulator metal (MIM) capacitor.

Emil Jakobsen

To evaluate each parameters the steps are taken:
1. The maximum bandwidth the CPPLL can have is set to 60kHz, due to the fact that the CPPLL can
have an input reference clock as low as 600kHz, so to make sure the system acts like a continuous
time system a factor of 10 is used as explained in section 4.5.2.
2. The gain of the VCO is found from equation 98 to be: KV CO = 0.28G rad
s·V . The maximum and
minimum input frequency has been added and subtracted 20% each respectively to make sure that
the VCO has the needed operation range even within PVT and process variations. The regulating
voltage is set to be from 1V to 0.4V which is expected to be able to create, however will set
constrains on the choice of charge pump. Preferably the control voltage is made larger to reduce
the VCO gain.
3. A damping factor is chosen to be one, which should give a response with low overshoot, explanation
in section 4.5.1). With the damping factor of one, the unity gain bandwidth can be approximated
to be equal to the open loop gain with within 3%.
4. With the damping factor and open loop gain K, the natural frequency can be found from equation
104, which gives ωn = 188 k·rad
s
Page 42 of 157
==PAGE:44==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
5. With the natural frequency and damping the lock range, pull-out range and lock time can be found
from equation 64, 63 and 72 respectively to be:
TL = 2 · π
ωn
= 33µs
(107)
∆ωP O = 11.55 · ωn · (ζ + 0.5) = 3.26 M · rad
s
(108)
∆ωH = 4 · π · ζ · ωn = 2.37 M · rad
s
(109)
6. The charge pump current is chosen to be 1µA since it is expected that this current will ﬂow
constantly (more on this in section 7.2). With a charge pump current of this value equation 104
can predict the size of C1, which will have a value of C1 = 315pF, where the division ratio is set
to N = 4.
7. Next the resistor R can be found from equation 105 to have the value of R = 33kΩ
8. The size of C2 is needed to be chosen, a ratio of
1
20 between C1 and C2 should give a phase margin
of 65◦which should be proper.
9. Last the -3dB bandwidth of the CPPLL can be found from equation 40 which gives a value of
74kHz with the found gain and damping. It should be noted it is expected to be larger than this
due to the peaking of the zero in the response, but gives an estimate of the value.
Now what happens with the division ratio change from 4 to 8. The damping factor will decrease to
ζ = 0.707, the natural frequency will decrease to ωn = 133 k·rad
s
, the lock time will increase to TL = 47µs,
the pull-out range will decrease to 1.86 M·rad
s
, the lock range will decrease to 1.18 M·rad
s
and the -3dB
frequency will fall to 43kHz. The unity gain bandwidth will decrease to 30kHz, however this does not
hold true since, due to the decrease in damping the real unity cutoﬀfrequency will be a bit higher than
this as explained in section 4.5.2. Also the phase margin will decrease, since the location of the pole and
zero will not move but the unity cutoﬀfreqency will, and the 65◦phase margin will be reduced. A plot
of phase margin versus change of unity gain frequency is shown in ﬁgure 29 created from equation 45.
Figure 29: Phase margin change in respect to changes in unity gain frequency of the CPPLL from
optimum
It can be seen from ﬁgure 29 that the CPPLL is not that sensitive to changes in the unity gain frequency.
Even though the unity gain frequency is change from 60kHz to 30kHz for a change in division, the phase
margin will only decrease to 57◦.

5.2
Implementation of behavioural model
The phase domain model now with all parameters calculated is implemented in cadence spectre. Pro-
grams like maple or matlab could also be used, but it is easier to implement the noise eﬀect on the model
in cadence.
Figure 30: View of phase domain model from cadence
In ﬁgure 30 the model that is build up in cadence is shown. The PFD, Charge pump and divider gain
block is made out of a gain stages (amp model from ahdlLib in cadence). Next the Low pass ﬁlter and
VCO is made out of polynomials sources (svcvs model from analogLib in cadence). The PFD gain block
has the gain of
1
2·π, the charge pump gain block has the gain of Icp = 1µA and the divider gain block
has the gain of
1
N . Next the Low pass ﬁlter block has the the polynomial as shown in equation 100
implemented as shown in ﬁgure 31 and the VCO block is created by the polynomial shown in equation
98 implemented as shown in ﬁgure 32. The boxes marked with noise models, is used later to simulated
how the noise contribution for each block will aﬀect the system. They are implemented by verilog-A
models that emulates white noise or ﬂicker noise or both together. More on this in section

To make sure that the system still is stable under process variations, a lower and upper limit of the unity
gain bandwidth is tested. It is assumed that the capacitors can vary with ±20%, the gain of the VCO
can vary ±3 times norminal value and the charge pump current can vary ±20%
Figure 36 shows worse case for lower limit of the unity bandwidth. The worse case for lowest unity
bandwidth will correspond to an 20% decrease and increase of C1 and C2 respectively, the VCO gain
has reduced by a factor of 3 and the charge pump current decreased by 20% and a division ratio of 8.
This will move the zero to higher frequencies and the pole to lower reducing the phase margin peak to
60◦, the damping factor to 0.36 and the lock time to 110µs. Further with the decrease of VCO and
charge pump gain the unity bandwidth will be reduces however with this low damping the assumption
that the dc gain of the CPPLL correspond to the unity bandwidth doesn’t hold anymore, however since
all parameters is known equation 42 can be used, which gives a unity bandwidth of 13kHz. From the
top plot it can be seen that the unity bandwidth has decreased to 13kHz and the phase margin to 32◦
so the system is still stable. In the middle plot the reduced damping factor causes large oscillation and
after 110µs which was the calculated lock time the CPPLL is within 5%, however shall be noted that a
one more peak after this is seen, which correspond to the same 5% so the system has a longer lock time
than calculated. In the bottom plot a large gain peak is seen as expected for this low damping and the
-3dB bandwidth is at 20kHz.

6
Freqyency synthesiser topology block by block
This section will look into what building blocks for the PLL that shall be used to achieve the desired
speciﬁcations speciﬁed in table 1.
6.1
Voltage controlled oscillator
The VCO is one of the most important if not the most important building block in a PLL. The VCO
aﬀects for example phase noise, timing jitter, duty cycle, frequency tuning range and stability. Oscillators
are often generalized as either resonant based / harmonic oscillators and non resonant based / relaxed
oscillator. The resonant based oscillator generates a sinusoidal waveform, an example of the principle
is an ampliﬁer with a phase shift network that produces a minimum of 180◦phase shift to generate
positive feedback, when the ampliﬁer is turned on the noise ﬂoor of the ampliﬁer is ampliﬁed and ﬁltered
and this loop continues until the the frequency set by the narrow-band ﬁlter is reach. This produces
a output frequency with low phase noise and spectral purity (inherent stability of the signal). Relaxed
oscillators is a type that changes between two stable states, by a nonlinear component, which normal
produces non sinusoidal wave forms, such as a square wave or triangle waveform. Since the switching of
a relaxed oscillator is rapidly between stable states is generated high amount of harmonic contents [7,29]
(The fast rising edge of a square wave contains many harmonics, this can be composed down to the
Fourier Series which the edge of the square wave contains a linear weighted sum of harmonically related
sinusoids [4, p. 226]).
Four main voltage controlled oscillators will be look into and performance compared to ﬁnd the best
suited application to fulﬁll the speciﬁcations seen in table 1.
6.1.1
Current starved ring osscillator
The current starved ring oscillator (CSVCO) works by the principal of having uneven numbers of inverters
(to fulﬁll the Barkhausen criterion [29, p. 22]) where the source and sink current available to each inverter
stage is controlled or the capacitance at each inverter node is changed. The frequency is then controlled
by the source/sink current in cooperation with the capacitance at the inverter node. The oscillation of
the CSVCO is given by:
f0 =
1
2 · M · Td
(111)
Where in equation 111 M is the number of stages and Td is the delay for each stage. The two in the
denominator comes from that two cycle around the inverter loop needs to be done to generate one full
clock cycle. The delay Td is created as mention above by the amount of current available to charge or
discharge the input capacitance of a inverter stage, if larger delay is needed extra capacitance can be
added as shown in ﬁgure 40.
Page 53 of 157
==PAGE:55==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Clk_vco
Vss
Figure 40: Block diagram of a 3 stage CSVCO
The preferred method of changing the frequency is by controlling the source/sink current available to
each stage, since capacitance tuning gives small tuning range, non linearity in gain and low maximum
frequency [7, p. 183].
If a closed look into the waveform between an inverter stage is looked at, the rising and falling edge
is slowly varying, as the aﬀect of the current is starved to each stage. This slow varying waveform is
sensitive to power supply ripple which makes it diﬃcult to generate a 50% duty cycle. Further if there
is mismatch between the source and sink current to each stage this will also aﬀect the duty cycle. To
generate a square wave output of the CSVCO a buﬀer stage is normal added. A CSVCO does also create
high current spikes in its operation when cross conduction in the inverter stages occur, this can cause
high frequency ripple in the power supply and the substrate [7, p. 186], this can however be reduces
by controlling the cross conduction and decouple the power supply with capacitance, to reduce voltage
ripple. If care is not taken a CSVCO can have a high non linearity it is gain (the output frequency versus
control voltage), this can make the PLL get unstable since the large gain change can reduce the phase
margin of the close loop. Furthermore at some point if the control voltages gets to low, the CSVCO will
stop oscillation and my not start again, which will need a restart of the PLL.
6.1.2
diﬀerential ring osscillator
In contrast to the single ended CSVCO a diﬀerential ring oscillator (DIFFVCO) can be made out of even
numbers of inverter stages however one stage the positive output of the DIFFVCO must be connected
to the negative input of the upcoming stage. It creates a close to 50% duty cycle, is able to create
quadrature output (90◦phase shift between two signals) and due to it is diﬀerential reject common
mode noise [7, p. 191]. The DIFFVCO suﬀer from the disadvantages reduce maximum output frequency
due to the extra capacitance that is needed to be charge and discharge because of the diﬀerential topology,
it produce a low output swing which if not careful design causes the diﬀerential to single ended output
stage maybe skip cycles and has larger die area because of bias generators [7, p. 191] and it has worse
phase noise than a CSVCO again because of diﬀerential topology.
The DIFFVCO can also be categorized into three categories: saturated (All transistors in delay cell
fully switch), unsaturated (some transistors in delay cell is on all the time, and some transistors fully
switched) and non saturated (All transistors in delay cell is on all the time) type. The saturated version
the output reached Vdd and unsaturated output never reaches Vdd [30] [31].A saturated type has better
phase noise performance due to most of the delay cells are turned oﬀduring cycle where they cannot
contribute to the noise in the whole VCO, where in contrasts a non saturated, some transistors is never
turned oﬀwhich will increase the phase noise, furthermore a large signal swing inside the VCO loop
also decreases the phase noise (large signal swing gives large signal power which increases signal to noise
ratio) [32]. A CSVCO is a saturated type, since it each delay cell switches to full rail, in comparison to
a non saturated DIFFVCO, due to this the CSVCO would have better phase noise performance [31].
Page 54 of 157
==PAGE:56==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Figure 41: Block diagram of a 4 stage DIFFVCO where the last stages output is swap before feed back
to the ﬁrst stage to make sure oscillation occurs
6.1.3
LC resonant oscillator
The basic principle of an LC resonator can be describe by ﬁgure 42. First a capacitor is charge up by
a source, then a switch change state and connects the capacitor and inductor in parallel. The capacitor
discharges through the inductor, which causes the current in the inductor to rise and the voltage on
the capacitor to fall. The increase current in the inductor creates an electromagnetic ﬁeld which repel
the ﬂow of current in it, when all charge from the capacitor as been discharge and converted to a
electromagnetic ﬁeld in the inductor, a back electromagnetic force is created which causes the current
from the inductor to ﬂow back into the capacitor and charging it, when the current in the inductor reach
zero and the capacitor is fully charge (however with opposite polarity) the cycle begins again. In an
ideal world this would keep on forever however due to losses the circuit will die out. In an integrated
LC resonator an ampliﬁer is connected to the LC tank, to provide start up and maintain the oscillation.
Three main types of LC resonant oscillators for integration they are the Colpitt, the Seiler and the Pierce
oscillator [33, p. 18] [7, p. 205].A LC resonant oscillator provides very low phase noise in the range of
up to 20dB lower than a CSVCO due to low switching transient in the LC tank and the high quality
factor [7, p. 213]. There is however some main disadvantages for LC resonant oscillators for integration,
and that is the inductor. To produce low frequencies the inductor size needs to be very large (if the
principle LC tank shown in ﬁgure 42 is used the oscillation frequency would be given by ωo =
1
√
L·C ), this
contains the LC resonator oscillator to high frequency applications in the GHz area, where the size of
the inductor will be reduced and reasonable quality factor can be achieved [34, p. 95-105]. Furthermore
a LC resonant oscillator need a high quality varactor to tune the frequency, where a integrated variable
capacitor is limited in the range which it can be tuned, thereby reducing the frequency bandwidth the
LC resonant oscillator can achieve [30].
Figure 42: Principle diagram of an LC tank.
Page 55 of 157
==PAGE:57==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
6.1.4
Multivibrator
A multivibrator works by the principle by charging and discharging a capacitor between to reference
voltage levels. A comparator can be used to switch switches when either the lower or upper reference
voltage level is reach to control the charging and discharging cycle. The output frequency of the mul-
tivibrator can then be controlled by either changing the current available for charging and discharging
or chancing the reference voltage level the multivibrator needs to charge up to or discharge down to.
A multivibrator has the advangdes it can create non overlapping clocks due to the regeneration time
of the comparator, it can achieve high speed operations and has high linearity. The disadvantages of a
multivibrator is high current spikes and a multi oscillation can occur inside the multivibrator due to the
comparator and an inverter can create a ring oscillator [7, p. 199].
Clk
Clk
Clk_B
Clk_B
Vout_A
Vout_B
Vss
Vdd
Figure 43: Principle diagram of a multivibrator. When CLK is connected the capacitor is charged, when
a threshold voltage is reach CLK disconnects and CLKB connects and discharge the capacitor until a
threshold is reach, then the ﬁrst cycle starts again.
6.1.5
VCO performance comparison and choice for PLL
Table 5 gives a comparison between some VCO performance parameters. It shall be noted than a CSVCO
has a bit better far out phase noise than a diﬀerential ring oscillator and a multivibrator.
