The choice of VCO is chosen to be the CSVCO over the other topologies. A LC tank would needed
a huge inductor to produce the low frequency the is speciﬁed (minimum output frequency of the VCO
is 2.4MHz given by speciﬁcation in table 1), the diﬀerential ring oscillator would have larger area than
a CSVCO, worse jitter performance due to higher phase noise, larger power consumption and it is not
critical to produce a 50% duty cycle for the design. Lastly the multivibrator is not picked due to the
extra needed circuitry to sense the state (comparator) to control it, where the CSVCO will provide
the needed wide tuning range and have lower power consumption. Further since jitter performance is
a relaxed speciﬁcation (see speciﬁcation in table 1), a CSVCO is assumed to be able to meet criteria
needed. Linearity is though needed to be looked at to make sure that the gain of the VCO doesn’t
change much over PVT and process variations, to make sure that the PLL will remain stable.
6.2
Charge pump and Operational ampliﬁer
The second most important building block is the charge pump (CP) / Operational ampliﬁer (Opamp),
this circuit with the phase detector will create the control voltage to the loop ﬁlter. The performance
parameters the CP / Opamp aﬀect is for example loop stability, jitter performance, phase error tracking,
frequency operation range [7, p. 280]. In this section the performance of using either a charge pump or
an operational ampliﬁer will be looked at.
6.2.1
Charge pump
A charge pump (CP) consist of two current sources either to sink or source current into the loop ﬁlter.
Which current source that is turned on is controlled by the up and down pulses from the phase detector.
CP have the advantages of using small area due to simple implementation and is capable of fast switching.
The disadvantages is however, if small bandwidth is needed that gain of the CP needs to be reduce. This
small gain (low current output) reduces SNR, furthermore the low gain reduces the sensitivity of pulse
width from the phase detector (PD), due to when switch is turned on to for example deliver current, some
charge is needed to charge parasitic capacitances and if the gain is low enough, all the potential output
current will be used to charge parasitics instead of the loop ﬁlter within a small pulse width from the PD.
Ideally the charge CP should have an inﬁnity output impedance (to act like an ideal integrator), however
this is not true for a real implemented CP which reduces is ability of tracking of error. Careful matching
is also needed for the charge and discharge current, if mismatch is present a static phase error will occur
proportional to the mismatch between up and down current which will increase jitter [7, p. 286].
6.2.2
Operational ampliﬁer
An operational ampliﬁer is another choice to create the ideal integrated, mimics the integrator by the
amount of gain instead of output impedance. Instead of moving a charge in and out of the loop ﬁlter as
the CP does, the operation ampliﬁer will create a voltage output proportional to the up and down pulses
from the PD. The Opamp aﬀect the same performance parameters as the CP, however it performance
better in the area of PSRR, Phase noise, jitter and dc gain which makes it look like more as an ideal
integrator which will reduce error tracking. However disadvantages over a CP is much larger current
consumption, since wide bandwidth and gain is needed for low noise and jitter and enough current
is needed for fast slew rate operation which is needed for fast switching time. If external loop ﬁlter
components also is needed the CP is also preferred since it will only need one output pad, where the
Opamp would need two (it needs two external capacitance versus the one the CP needs). The Opamp
can be realized by many diﬀerent topologies depending on what is needed, for example a folded cascode
which will give good gain but reduce output swing (reduces frequency operation range of the PLL), a
class B ampliﬁer which have even higher gain, slewrate and output swing [7, p. 286-297].
Page 57 of 157
==PAGE:59==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
6.2.3
Choice between charge pump and operational ampliﬁer
The CP is picked over the Opamp, due to the much lower current consumption realisation, even though
it performance worse on the parameters of looking like an ideal integrator which improves error tracking
and the phase noise within the bandwidth of the frequency synthesizer [7, p. 301]. However with the
loose criteria for the jitter it should be possible to create a CP that can live up to the speciﬁcations given
in table 1.
6.3
Phase detector
The PD is used to compare the reference frequency with the divided VCO frequency, by comparing their
phase. For a frequency synthesizer also the frequency is needed to be compared, due to if a PD is used
it can lock onto wrong frequencies, since it only detects the phase. An example could be for a 600kHz
reference frequency a PD would lock onto 1.2MHz instead of the desired 2.4Mhz (if the VCO clock was
divided by four), if it is tracking from a low frequency and up. A PD can be used for a frequency
synthesizer but then a acquisition circuit is also needed. A PFD doesn’t need a acquisition aid since it
is able to detect both phase and frequency diﬀerent, due to its inﬁnity pull in range as explain in section
4.6.2. However one place where a PFD isn’t suitable and a PD with a acquisition aid would be preferred
is clock recovery, since multiple false lock conditions can occur and missing clock edges which would
confuse the PFD [7, p. 276]. To realize the frequency synthesizer in this project a PFD is used.
6.4
Divider
The divider in frequency synthesizer is used to scale down the frequency from the VCO, before it is
feed back to the PD. By scaling down the VCO frequency a multiplication of the reference frequency by
the division ratio can be achieved. The divider should be faster than the maximum output frequency
of the VCO, this is because if the divider is slower than the update rate of the VCO it can get lost.
Meaning that it doesn’t follow the VCO, which in the loop can cause the control voltage to go to either
rail [7, p. 164]. Diﬀerent types of divider can be used for the speciﬁc needs, a integer divider divides
the VCO frequency down by integers of two, if fractional division is needed a fractional divider can be
used, if multiple diﬀerence frequency outputs is needed from the VCO a programmable divider can be
used. For the speciﬁcations in table 1 a division of 4 and 8 is needed, this can be made quit simple with
a integer divider with a multiplexer. So further detailed look into divider topologies will be made.
One note to the divider is that cycle slip can occur when the division ratio goes from low to high, which
increases the lock time. Cycle slip occurs when the divider skips a cycle which will cause the CP to
lower the control line voltage instead of increasing it, and thereby increasing the lock time, this can be
prevented by increasing the loop bandwidth if possible [15, p. 37].
6.5
Loop ﬁlter
The loop ﬁlter in the frequency synthesizer with the CP/Opamp creates the control voltage to the VCO.
Great detail of passive ﬁlters has been covered, and a short mention of active ﬁlters also in section 4.4.5.
The passive ﬁlter is preferred when low noise is desired since it only contributes with thermal noise
where as said in section 4.4.5 also contributes with ﬂicker noise. The active loop ﬁlter also causes a dc
oﬀset, which will cause a phase oﬀset, which from section 4.9 was found will increase the reference spurs.
However as mention before the active ﬁlter can sometimes be needed if the passive ﬁlter with the CP
doesn’t have large enough tuning range.
Page 58 of 157
==PAGE:60==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
7
Literature studies to ﬁnd the topologies for the PLL
Numerous papers and books have been studied to ﬁnd the preferred topology to implement the speciﬁc
block, and what restrictions each blocks gives to each other. For example VCO would set the restriction
to the design of the CP design, in the sense what output voltage should the CP be able to cover. The
PFD should be able to cancel out deadzone in cooperation with the CP. This section will state which
topology that was selected.
7.1
Current starved ring oscillator
The main quest was to ﬁnd a topology for a current starved ring oscillator (CSVCO) that minimizes the
non linearity that a CSVCO gain suﬀers from. This change in gain over its tuning range, could remove
the high phase margin design for, and make the CPPLL unstable.
In the book [35] a proposed CSVCO with high linearity is presented, it present a large signal analysis of
how to improve the linearity by keeping the bias transistor in saturation (transistor M13 shown in ﬁgure
44), more on the speciﬁc design in section 8.2.
Figure 44: Suggested CSVCO.
7.2
Charge pump
After the VCO was design it was found that a quit wide range of output voltage was needed for the
control voltage. Further since it is desired to use as low current consumption as possible, the switching
of the CP is a concern. To maintain high switching speed the current ﬂow can’t be turned oﬀin the
output stage, so a dummy load is needed. Since switching is a problem for low current consumption is
that if the CP output is turned oﬀ, there will go some time before it will be able to deliver the correct
output current, since the output stage parasitic capacitance needs to be charged ﬁrst, second the turn
on time also gives constrains on the PFD in how long the reset delay shall be, to get rid of dead zone.
Last a high output impedance is desired for the CP to emulate a ideal integrator as best as possible to
reduce static error.
Many CP topology use a cascode current mirror as the output or a regulated cascode output as in [36],
to increase the output resistance and when the cascode current mirror get press in voltage range, when
either the control voltage get high or low and reduces the VDS voltage over it, which forces the transistors
in the cascode current mirror to go into triode region, an error ampliﬁer is usally used to make sure that
the VDS of the top/bottom current mirror is ﬁxed, to reduce current mismatch. The circuit seen in 45
is a redraw of the topology used in [36], which shows the regulated cascode output and error ampliﬁers
Page 59 of 157
==PAGE:61==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
as describe above, to reduce output current mismatch, where M25, M26 and M29 created a regulated
cascode for the sink current, and M13, M14, M21, M22 and M12 creates a diﬀerential ampliﬁer to make
sure that the VDS over M26 matches VDS over M19 also to reduce current mismatch as describe above.
Shall be noted this circuit was not used, but is inserted to help the reader get an idea how the cascode
output stage with error ampliﬁer works. The reason this topology wasn’t uses is as due to the reduce
output voltage swing due to cascode ouput, and low switching speed since the output it turn oﬀ, when
UP and DOWN pulses from the PFD is not present.
Figure 45: Redrawn circuit from the paper [36] to explain how the regulated cascode output with error
ampliﬁers work.
The topology that was used for the CP in this design is adapted from [37] and shown in ﬁgure 46.
Page 60 of 157
==PAGE:62==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Figure 46: Principle drawing of the topology used in this design, adapted from [37].
The circuit in ﬁgure 46 work by the principle that a bias current created by the current source IBias, will
make a voltage drop over resistor R1 and R3. When UP and DOWN signal is present and switch S1 and
S2 is closed and switch S3 and S4 is open, the voltage on the control line is connected to resistor R2 and
R4 through the variable resistor connected to the output of the operational ampliﬁer. The operational
ampliﬁers will force the voltage over R2 and R4 to be equal to the voltage over resistor R1 and R4
respectively, by adjusting the resistance value of the variable resistors. This will create the relation for
the sink and source current by:
Isource = Ibias · R3
R4
(112)
Isink = Ibias · R1
R2
(113)
7.3
Phase frequency detector
To get rid of dead zone the PFD should be able to react to very small phase diﬀerence between the
reference and VCO frequency. The standard PFD consist of two D-ﬂip ﬂops and a and/nand gate and
some delay cells [7, p. 268] and seen in ﬁgure 47.
Page 61 of 157
==PAGE:63==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Figure 47: Principle drawing of the PFD topology used in this design.
This eliminates deadzone by having minimum pulses width out of both up and down signal even when no
phase or frequency error is present, by delaying the reset signal to the d-ﬂip ﬂops. This does that it isn’t
the propagation delay in the logic that determine the smallest phase/frequency diﬀerence it can detect.
When phase/frequency diﬀerence is occurring this error will be added to the minimum pulse width the
PFD creates. Other methods have been seen to reduce the dead zone, the paper [38] reduces the amount
of logic needed for the error signal to propagate through (two gate delays) and from the paper [39] (three
gate delays). The reduce amount of logic and very small minimum output pulse is created which reduce
the power consumption. However when very small output pulses is created, a restriction to the CP is
made, since it should be able to react to these small pulses, since for example a 100ps phase diﬀerence
between the reference and VCO would create a 100ps pulse, which the CP should be able to turn on to,
to minimize the phase error. If it is not possible to do this, the correction would ﬁrst occur when the
phase diﬀerence is large enough so the CP can react to it and this eﬀect creates a deadzone also. Since
the CP that is made is using a small source/sink current, a limitation to the minimum pulse width is
set. So the standard PFD is preferred which has the build in minimum pulse width by delaying the reset
signal.
7.4
Loop ﬁlter components
No deeper study was made in for the loop ﬁlter components. It was decided to use a passive ﬁlter, so
only concern was if the capacitance should be made out of mos capacitor or mim capacitors. Since no
area restriction for the design was made, mim capacitor was chosen due to their lower leakage current
compared to mos capacitors, which will help suppress reference spurs and phase oﬀset as explained in
section 4.9.
7.5
Divider
As for the loop ﬁlter no deep study was made on how to create the integer by 2 divider, since is it is
simple made out of d-ﬂip ﬂops and a multiplexer for this project sine only two division ratios is needed.
If multiples diﬀerence divisions would be needed, a programmable divider would be prefer [7, p. 164],
however this it not the case.
Page 62 of 157
==PAGE:64==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
8
Block Implementation
In this section each block is designed for the CPPLL, where each sub section gives a detailed description
of who the design process was done. It is assumed for all design that a well regulated voltage supply
is available with good PSRR, so supply noise isn’t taken into account, further process invariant supply
current is also available.
There isn’t an area limitation, however the design of the individual block is done with low current
consumption in mind.
8.1
Transistor characterization
A 130nm process design kit (PDK) was used for the design of the CPPLL. Process speciﬁcations was
given however the gain factor was not speciﬁed, so before design of each individual block in the CPPLL
the gain factor was found by using the following setup shown in ﬁgure 48.
Figure 48: Test setup for transistor characterization

a parametric analyses was created where VGS was swept from 0.8V to 1.2V in 100mV steps, where VSG
is set equal to VGS and the Width is set to 5µm and the length to 1µm for both the Nmos and Pmos
seen in ﬁgure 48. From this the following plot was created:

8.2
Voltage controlled oscillator
The current starved voltage controlled oscillator (CSVCO) should be design so peaking in the response
can accounted for, so the range of which the CSVCO shall be able to oscillate has to be increase for both
the lower and upper end (peaking due to the zero in the transfer function, see section 4.4.5). Further
the extra range is needed to give margin for PVT variations, so the minimum and upper limit of the
frequency needed is still covered. The minimum output frequency of the VCO will be the minimum input
frequency which is 600kHz multiplied by the lowest division ratio which is four, and maximum frequency
the VCO shall covor is the maximum input frequency which is 3MHz times the highest division ratio
which if 8. Further extra margin is added for both the upper and lower range, chosen to be 20%. The
oscillation frequency of a CSVCO is given by:
fosc =
Ibias
N · Ctot · Vdd
(116)
Where in equation 116 Ibias is the biasing current for the inverter stage Ctot is the total capacitance in
a single stage and Vdd is the supply voltage. The total sum of capacitance for one inverter stage is given
by:
Ctot = 5
2 · Cox · (W2 · L2 + W3 · L3)
(117)
Where W2 and L2 is the width and the length of transistor M2 seen in ﬁgure 50 and W3 and L3 is the
width and length of transistor M3 in ﬁgure 50.
Figure 50: Single inverter stage of CSVCO
Page 65 of 157
==PAGE:67==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Leesons equation 85 modiﬁed to a CSVCO [7, p. 189] is:
L(f) =

1 + fl
f
 10
Namp
10
V 2
os
10
Gn
10
·
 
1 +

fc
2 · QL
· 1
f
2!
(118)
Where in equation 118 fl is the ﬂicker corner frequency in Hz, Vos is the voltage swing inside the inverter
stage in V , Ql is the quality factor of the resonator, fc is the center frequency in Hz, Gn is the gain
of the ampliﬁer in dB and Namp is the equivalent input noise of the ampliﬁer in the oscillator in dBc
Hz .
From equation 118 it can be seen to reduce the far from carrier phase noise one can lower the gain of
the ampliﬁer (the delay cell), increase the width of the inverter stage and maximize the voltage swing,
while increasing the length of the inverter stage can reduce the ﬂicker corner frequency. The Q factor
cannot be aﬀected for a ring oscillator that it is always one, since no energy is stored in each cycle, since
the charge that is stored on the rising edge is discharge again on the falling edge.
Even though is desired to have a large length for the inverter stage to reach the speciﬁcation of a phase
noise of 95 dBc
Hz at 10kHz and 20kHz, it should be kept as small as possible. This is due to it is desired to
use as small current consumption as possible, which from equation 116 means that the total capacitance
needs to be small, due to the low maximum output frequency of the CSVCO of 28.8MHz (20% higher
than the maximum frequency of 24MHz). Since the capacitance will be low it is desired to only use the
inverters as the main capacitance to reduce noise by have maximum area of them. If a minimum bias
current is set to be 4µA for the high output frequency, and the supply voltage is nominal 1.45V and
the number of stages is set to N=3 then the total capacitance Ctot shall be 33fF from equation 117.
From the PDK that is used the oxide capacitance of a 1.5V device is 11 fF
µm2 , so the total area of the
inverter transistors shall be 2.9µm. To make sure it isn’t the inverters that limit the current they shall
minimum be able to handle 4µA, which set a restriction on the width and length relationship. Further
the switching away from depletion region shall be fast since the capacitance a mosfet isn’t static, it varies
with the applied gate to bulk voltage [12, p. 59], so this change in capacitance will eﬀect the linearity.
With this is mind the length of transistor M2, and M3 is set to 390nm, so from equation 117 the width
of transistor M2 and M3 can be found to be W2 = 1.5µm and W3 = 6µm. The width of M3 is made
about 4 times larger than M2, to create the most symmetrically wavefrom inside the CSVCO, due to
diﬀerent gain factor of M2 and M3. Next the dimension of M1 and M4 shall be found seen in ﬁgure
50 and ﬁgure 51. To ensure linearity M1 should be kept in saturation over the desired control voltage
range. It is decided that the control voltage for the minimum current to be 450mV which will create the
low output frequency, since it is assumed it is feasible to create the charge pump that can have this low
an output voltage.
Page 66 of 157
==PAGE:68==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Figure 51: Bias stage for the CSVCO
To maximize the voltage range where transistor M1 and M4 can be kept in saturation, low threshold
voltage transistors is used. M1’s width is set to minimum of 150nm and the length for M1 is solved for
by the equation [12, p. 26], for an output drain current of 400nA:
Id = 1
2 · µCox · W
L · (Vgs −Vt)2
(119)
From equation 119 the width of M1 is found to be 1.61µm. M4 is diode coupled so it will always be in
saturation until VGS gets below the threshold voltage, where it will go into subthreshold region, this will
reduce the linearity of the CSVCO however it is M1 that aﬀects the linearity the most. A low Veﬀvoltage
for M4 is desired to keep M1 in saturation for large control voltages, it is predicted that over PVT that
the size of maximum control voltage will get over the 900mV. If it is assumed that the control voltage
is some cases needs to get as high as 1.1V to create the fast frequency for example in the slow process
corner for the nmos, then the Veﬀvoltage of M4 shall be lower than 200mV. If the length of M4 is set
to be 390nm and solved for a 150mV eﬀective voltage with a drain current of 6µA to accommodate for
larger capacitance in some process corners for the inverters, then the width can be solve from: [12, p. 27]:
Veff =

equation 120 can be solve for when it reaches 0V.

8.3
Charge pump
The proposed CP from [37] shown as principle drawing is shown in ﬁgure 46 is created by the circuit
shown in ﬁgure 52.
The total current consumption is design for to be 5µA in typical process corner.
Figure 52: Top level schematic of used charge pump where resistors in ﬁgure 46 is change out with the
implemented transistors
Page 68 of 157
==PAGE:70==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
The output impedance of the CP is given by [37]:
Rout = (1 + gm · R1 · A0) · rout + rds1
(121)
Where gm and rout is the transconductance and output impedance of M7 and M5, A0 is the DC gain
of the operational ampliﬁer, rds1 is the drain source resistance from M1, and R1 is the tail resistance
of M9 and M11. Since M9 and M11 is used as switches they should be made small which will give low
tail resistance, further the size of M5 and M7 should also be kept low, this is due to reducing parasitic
capacitance, since when either the UP or DOWN signal is turned on, a capacitive charge injection from
the control line will be injected onto the output of the ampliﬁer, which for low power consumption
doesn’t have that high of slew rate to compensate for that change in voltage, and since the output node
of the ampliﬁer is a high impedance node, even small charge injection will move the voltage level. The
resistance of M1-M4 is also limited in size due to higher resistance of M1-M4 creates larger voltage drop
over them which decreases the dynamic operation range of the CP. So the best way to increase the output
impedance of the CP is by having very high gain ampliﬁers.
One shall note that M5 to M8 is choosen to be 3.3V devices instead of the 1.5V devices. The higher
threshold voltage of the 3.3V moves the cascode voltage up which helps the limited output range of the
telescopic ampliﬁer. The length would else have to be made very large for M5 and M8 to create large
enough VGS voltage
Transistor M13 to M16 is added to reduce charge injection to minimize spurs. M13 and M14 will cancel
charge injection for when UP and UP N goes in opposite direction, and M15 and M16 does the same
operation for the DOWN signal. The inverted UP and DOWN signal is made out of standard inverter
cells. The resistors that was seen in the principal schematic of the CP shown in ﬁgure 46 is made out of
M1 to M4, all connected so they are in triode region, so the output resistance of them can be calculated
from:
rds =
L
µCox · W · Veff
(122)
Since it is known that the control voltage for the CSVCO needs level as low as 360mV and as high as
1,166V, the CP needs to cover this range. Since there will be a small voltage drop over both transistor
M5,M7,M9 and M11 the voltage drop over M1-M4 can’t be to large. The voltage drop over M1-M4 is
set to 100mV, which gives margin for the voltage drop. A current of 1µA is decide to run through each
branch, to increase the switching speed of the CP. The larger size of the current running in each branch
the smaller the pulses from the PFD it can operate on. This is due to when either the UP or DOWN
branch is turned on to source or sink current, the current ﬂowing through M2 and M4 needs to charge
parasitic capacitances ﬁrst, before the desired size of the source or sink current can ﬂow into the control
line, so for very small current running in the branch almost no current will ﬂow into the loop ﬁlter. The
size of M1 to M4 can be found from equation 122, where the width of M1 to M4 is set to 300nm then
the length of M1 and M2 can be found to be 8µm where the value for Vt and the gain factor can be seen
in table 6, the same thing can be done for M3 and M4 where the length is found to be 2µm. Transistor
M9-M12 is made minimum size to achieve fast switching. Next the ampliﬁers shall be design.

8.3.1
Ampliﬁer
For the ampliﬁer, the paper [37] used a telescopic ampliﬁer with level shifted inputs, due to the low
input voltages to the ampliﬁer. The telescopic ampliﬁer has the advantages of high switching speed
and low power consumption, disadvantages are limited output swing due to stacking transistors [40],
and design diﬃculties for low supply voltage again due to stacking of transistors. The topology for a
telescopic ampliﬁer is shown in ﬁgure 53, it consist of an active load made out of a wide swing current
mirror consisting of transistor M5 to M8, a diﬀerential input pair consisting of M1 and M2, a tail current
created by M9 and a cascode stage consisting of M3 and M4. The active load can be made out of a
cascode current mirror instead of the wide swing to eliminate the cascode biasing of M5 and M6, however
this will further reduce output voltage swing, since a cascode current mirror limit the output voltage
by 2 · Veff + Vt [12, p. 130] where the wide swing current mirror shown in ﬁgure 53 only limits by
2 · Veff [12, p. 262].
Page 70 of 157
==PAGE:72==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
Figure 53: Principal diagram of a telescopic ampliﬁer
The DC gain of the topology shown in ﬁgure 53 is given by:
A0 = gm1 ·
 rds8 · rds6 · rds2 · rds4 · gm6 · gm4
rds8 · rds6 · gm6 + rds2 · rds4 · gm4

(123)
Where gm is the transconductance of each individual transistor and rds is the drain to source resistance
of each individual transistor. Since low current consumption is in focus, which sets a limit to the size of
the transconductance for transistors in saturation which can be found from [12, p. 27]:
gm =
r
2 · µCox · W
L · ID = 2 · µCox · W
L · Veff = 2 · Id

The reason there is a limit to size of the transconductance for the transistors that needs to be in saturation
the the size of the eﬀective voltage smaller than VDS but large enough so they don’t go into subthreshold.
Large transistors is needed to achieve high dc gain with the low power consumption which increases rds.

rds ≈

current mirror with pmos input pair. The full schematic of the two telescopic ampliﬁers is shown in

The telescopic ampliﬁer shown in ﬁgure 55 was design ﬁrst. A tail current of 500nA for the ampliﬁer
was chosen. Since the power supply is speciﬁed to get as low as 1.4V not all transistors can be kept in
saturation, since not enough headroom is present, especially if Vout should have any output swing. It is
chosen that the input par M1 and M2 in subthreshold to get highest value of transconductance [12, p. 45].
The wide swing current mirror consisting of M5-M8 is design to be in saturation and also the tail transistor
M9 to be in saturation to have good current matching and as large Veﬀfor M5-M9 to reduce current
mismatch due to variation of threshold voltage [12, p. 101]. M3 and M4 is design to be in subthreshold.
The reason why the second cascode of M3 and M4 is design to be in subthreshold is due to the low power
supply, where if the output voltage gets to low, the VDS drop over M4 will get very low, and chancel all
gain. For a mosfet with a ﬁxed drain current, the transconductance in subthreshold is given by:
gmsub =
q · Id
n · k · T
(127)
Where in equation 127 q is electronic charge given by q = 1.602·10−19coulomb, k is Boltzmanns’ constant
given by k = 1.3806488 · 10−23 joule
K , T is the temperature in Kelvin and n is the strong inversions slope
factor given in the range of 1.2 to 1.6.
The tail transistor M9 is design to have a Veff voltage of 100mV, the width is set to 300nm and equation
119 is used to solve for L with a drain current of 100nA which will be multiplied up to 500nA by having
a multiplier of 5. With equation 119 the length of M9 is found to be 4.3µm. Next it is desired to have
about 250mV on the node V1 in ﬁgure 55 to give headroom before M9 will be pressed on VDS voltage,
the Veff of M1 to M4 is set to 50mV to have the in subthreshold, this means that to have 250mV on
node V1 the gate voltage on M1 and M2 shall be about 650mV. The drain current equation for a mosfet
in subthreshold is given by [12, p. 43-44]:
ID(sub) = ID0 · W
L · e
q·Veff
n·k·T
(128)
Page 73 of 157
==PAGE:75==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen

k · T
q
2
(129)
The size of M1 and M2 can be solved from equation 128 by setting Veff equal to 50mV, ID(sub) equal
to 250nA and setting the length to 2µm for a starting point for the rds resistance and solving for W,
which gives a width of 1.44 µm. The voltage at V2 in ﬁgure 55 is set to 150mV higher than the input
at M1, to create a 150mV voltage drop over M1 and M2 to ensure enough VDS voltage. This voltage
due to time constrains is made by and ideal source, a bias circuit should be made for this in the future.
M3 and M4 has the same size as M1 and M2 to create the 50mV Veff. The wide swing current mirror
is design by the procedure explain in [12, p. 262].
Figure 56: Wide swing current mirror adopted from [12, p. 262]
To ensure that the current mirror stays in saturation one has to ensure that transistor M3 threshold
voltage always is larger than the selected Veff voltage. Since the threshold voltage can get quit low
over PVT and it is expected also to be able to have a large output voltage swing the eﬀective voltage
is selected to be 100mV. The n ratio is selected to be one, and the bias current running in M5 is set to
100nA, so the length needs to be scaled further up than the four times that normally would be needed
for a n equal to one. The length of the transistors is made large to improve the output impedance, to
increase the gain. The length is set to 18 µm for M5 to M8 and the width is solve for using equation
119, where the drain current is set to half of the bias current, which gives a width of 14.5 µm. The
size of M12 is set to the same size of M7/M8 to make sure bulk modulation is the same for M11 as for
M5/M6, and M11 is sized 6 times lower than M5/M6, for time for the chosen n ratio of one, and 1.5
times more due to the lower bias current running though that branch, so the length is keept the same
size as M5/M6 and the width is scaled down 6 times to 2.4 µm. The size of M10 is set to M9 but only
with one multiply. The bias current chosen for the level shifters is set to 50nA, so the length of M14 and
M16 is kept the same as M11 and the width is scaled 2 times down compared to M11, to 1.2 µm. Last
M13 and M15 shall level shift from 100mV to 650mV so the gate to source voltage should be 500mV.
The width is set to 300nm and with a bias current of 50nA the length can be solve for with equation
119 to be 3µm.
The same design procedure was done for the ampliﬁer for the source current, and the sizes are presented

Phase frequency detector
A three stage PFD is used as shown in ﬁgure 57
Page 75 of 157
==PAGE:77==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier

Figure 57: Block diagram of a phase locked loop
It consist of two D ﬂip ﬂops and a NAND gate plus a delay element to delay the reset pulse to eliminate
dead zone.
UP = 0
DOWN = 0
UP = 0
DOWN = 1
UP = 1
DOWN = 0
Ref

Ref
VCO

VCO
Figure 58: State diagram of PFD
The PFD functions as seen in the state diagram in ﬁgure 58. When the reference and VCO has the
same frequency and phase the PFD is in the middle state where UP or DOWN signal is zero. If The
VCO’s phase or frequency leads the reference, the PFD will pulse the DOWN signal, and return to idle
state when the VCO and reference has same frequency and phase again. The oppesite happens if the
references phase or frequency leads the VCO, then the UP signal is pulsed until the reference and VCO
has equal phase and frequency again. One note is that in the middle stage, there will be UP and DOWN
pulses, to eliminate the dead zone, with a pulse width equal to the amount of delay added to the reset
path. Figure 59 to 62 shows the operation of the PFD for the diﬀerent scenarios.

Figure 62: Plot of functionality of the PFD where the reference phase leads the references, due note that
the UP signal is active low
The design of the PFD was done with standard logic cells, however they where made manually from a
netlist description since, no standard cell library was available. One modiﬁcation was however done to
Page 78 of 157
==PAGE:80==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
the D ﬂip ﬂop for the DOWN signal. Since QN comes before Q, and the UP signal comes from QN and
the DOWN signal from Q, the DOWN signal should be modiﬁed to match the QN as much as possible,
to make sure that when in the tristate state, where the reference and VCO is equal in frequency, the
minimum output pulses that exist to eliminate dead zone should be equal in period, so no static phase
error will occur, due to mismatch in the amount of charge and discharge current the CP will create, due
to mismatch of the pulse width. However even though modiﬁcation will be done one inverter delay will
always persist in diﬀerent between the two signal.
A standard output inverters width/length size is, Wp = 640nm, Wn = 420nm, Lp = 130nm and
Ln = 130nm where the sub notation of p is for Pmos, and n for Nmos. The output inverter for the
Q signal for the DOWN signal was modiﬁed to a size of Wp = 2µm, Wn = 300nm, Lp = 130nm and
Ln = 130nm. This was done to increase the rate it turns on, and decrease the time it turns oﬀ. No
calculation was done here, it was done by ﬁne tuning in the simulator in cadence spectre.
The delay added in the reset path was set to 1ns since the mismatch between the source and sink current
in the CP, below 1ns was evaluated to be to large, which would increase the static phase error.
8.5
Divider
An asynchronous divider was created by a ripple counter as seen in ﬁgure 63. It consist of three standard
D-ﬂip-ﬂop where each Q output clocks the next D-ﬂip-ﬂop and the QN signal is set back to each individual
D input. This conﬁguration creates divide by 2 conﬁguration, where each stage will divide the incoming
clock frequency by 2. A multiplexer is connected between the divide by 4 and divide by 8 output so the
division ratio can be selected.
Figure 63: Diagram of the asynchronous divider
It was decided to use an asynchronous divide even though it will add some jitter to the output of the
CPPLL, but it was estimated the amount of jitter added would be quit small since the division ratio is
so low [41], which minimize the accumulated jitter from the divider. The beneﬁt of the asynchronous
divider is that current can be saved due to each D-ﬂip-ﬂop will not operate on the maximum output
frequency of the VCO.
8.6
Loop ﬁlter
The loop ﬁlter is implemented by mim capacitors and polysilicon resistors. The max size of a mim cap
in the given process is 15pF so a multiplier of 21 is used for the large capacitor of C1, and the value of
C2 is set to the 13.5pF. The resistor is made out of 1k polysilicon resistor with a multiplier of 33 to get
the desired 33kΩresistance.
Page 79 of 157
==PAGE:81==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
9
Simulations results
This section contains all the simulation results from the CPPLL implemented and simulated in spectre
virtuoso. Typical values and summery of results will be presented in this section and all collected corner
simulations results is presented in section 12, and all monte carlo results is for 3 sigma variation. One
shall note that errors can occur in tables, since all data is manually transfer into the report. All values
in the tables is created by extractions.
Final note regarding simulations, is that PSS and PNOISE simulations has caused problems for the corner
simulations, due to the PSS simulation the designer sets a fundamental frequency in respect to the control
voltage, however over PVT the fundamental frequency isn’t ﬁxed for a given control voltage. So each
corner was needed to be run independently ﬁrst with a transient to ﬁnd the fundamental frequency and
then set this, however it stills moves over temperature, so the reader will see that the frequency of the
tstab isn’t a steady value of either the 2.4MHz or 24MHz but moves around this. This will gives results
that is either a bit worse or better than the desired tstab frequency of either 2.4MHz or 24MHz.

section will show typical simulations waveforms and present a table with summery of corner and monte
carlo simulations. Simulation data for each speciﬁc corner case is presented in section 12.1.

Figure 65: Simulation result for tuning and gain curve vs control voltage for typical corner. Simulation
results is created from a PSS simulations, with the setups shown in ﬁgure 95. It can be seen from the
top plot which shows tuning curve vs control voltage, that to create 2.4MHz output frequency which
is the lowest needed produced, a control voltage of approx 468mV is needed, and for the higest needed
frequency of the 24MHz a control voltage of approx 924mV is needed. The lower plot which shows
the vco gain vs control voltage, which is produces by diﬀerentiate the tuning curve, the gain over the
frequency range has a gain diﬀerence of approx 3.35.
The simulations result for the tuning, gain curve vs control voltage is shown in ﬁgure 65. The simulation
was done with a periodic steady state (PSS) simulation in cadence virtuoso on the testbench shown in
ﬁgure 105. The top plot shows what frequency will be produce in typical corner for a given control
voltage. The bottom plot shows the gain vs control voltage for the VCO, this plot was created by dif-
ferentiating the top plot. It can be see that the gain varies by a maximum of 3.35 times for the high
and low frequency that needs to be produced. Which as predicted from the behavioral model will aﬀect
the phase margin, due to the unity gain frequency will move from its optimum place, however as shown
from the behavioral model this can be accepted. It can be seen from both the tuning plot and the gain
plot that good linearity is achieved.
The simulation results for the phase noise, jitter og output noise of the VCO is shown in ﬁgure 66 to
ﬁgure 69 all simulated in typical corner. Where ﬁgure 66 shows the phase noise and jitter for the low
frequency of 2.4MHz, ﬁgure 67 shows the phase noise and jitter result for the high frequency of 24MHz,
ﬁgure 68 shows the output noise for the low frequency of 2.4MHz and ﬁgure 69 shows the output noise
for the high frequency at 24MHz. It can be seen even though the phase noise from the high frequency is
higher than for the low frequency, the jitter has reduces for the high frequency. This is due to the lower
period of the oscillation frequency which from equation 92 the jitter should be reduced.

very little each time, which will cause further phase oﬀsets and spurs. A note should be mention that it
is not statically 50nA over the range, it starts higher than this approximately 75nA and falls down to
50nA.

Table 15: Summery of typical and corner simulations for the PFD and CP in idle state, ie reference and
VCO clock is equal in phase and frequency
The results in table 15 is extracted from typical and corner simulations. The current in the resistors
seen in the top four rows is extracted from the current ﬂowing though them when no current is sink or
source from the CP. The average up current vs delay and average down current vs delay, is extracted by
measuring the average current ﬂowing though M7 and M5 in ﬁgure 52 during the minimum pulse created
from the PFD, where average up current vs delay, is measured in respect to the UP pulse width, and
average down current vs delay is measured in respect to the DOWN pulse width. The large percentage
error is both due to the UP and DOWN pulse isn’t the same pulse width and further from mismatch in
the source and sink current. The average output current of CP UP signal and average output current of
CP DOWN signal, is extracted by measuring the current ﬂowing out/in the CP during the pulse width
of the UP and DOWN signal respectively. The leakage current is extracted when no DOWN or UP pulse
is present, and last the average output current is measured of how much current there is sourced or sink
out of the CP during the minimum pulse width created by the PFD.

Table 21: Actual dimensions of transistors inside the source ampliﬁer after optimization
The veriﬁcation of the ampliﬁers was done with the testbenches shown in ﬁgure 102 and 104 for the source
and sink ampliﬁers respectively. Only DC and STB simulations was done, since noise and transient
response wasn’t of concern. From the STB simulation the plot in ﬁgure 75 was produced when the
ampliﬁer for the source path was connected in the CP, and no load capacitance was connected. It can be
observed that the conﬁguration the ampliﬁer is in created multiple poles and zeroes. To ensure stability
and phase margin, a 4pF capacitor is connected to the output of the ampliﬁer to set a dominant pole with
created by the output impedance of the ampliﬁer and the inserted capacitor. The large capacitor was
needed to make the dominante pole low enough in frequency to make sure the zero cross over frequency
occur before the large dip in phase seen at approximate 300kHz. The result of the loop gain and loop
phase is seen in ﬁgure 76 with the 4pF capacitor connected at the output of the ampliﬁer. The same
procedure was done for the sink ampliﬁer.

Figure 76: Simulation result for the output noise of the VCO for the low frequency of 24MHz at typical

Table 22 and 23 shows the summery of the extracted DC operation points and STB results for typical
and corner simulations. It can be observed that the source ampliﬁer has reduce gain in some corners
and the tail transistor goes out of saturation into triode. The reader shall note that in cadence virtuoso
the number one for region corresponds to triode, two corresponds to saturation and three corresponds
to subthreshold. The low gain is assumed to corresponds to that the wide swing current mirror gets
squeezed in VDS voltage due to the low output voltage, that is needed in some corners. This can be
corrected if the transistor M7 in ﬁgure 52 gets its length increased so the output level doesn’t need to
go this low. This was however not done and checked du to time constrains.

9.3
Top level results
The top level simulation of the CPPLL was done with the testbench shown in ﬁgure 97. The simulation
was set to run for 1.2ms where the division ratio was at the start set to 4, at 0.4ms the division was
change to 8 by the multiplexer in the divider, then at 0.8ms the division was set to 4 again, this cycle was
made to make sure the CPPLL could go into lock in both cases. In ﬁgure 77 the control voltage for the
VCO is shown for a reference frequency of 600kHz, it can be seen that the CPPLL regulates the control
voltage down to needed voltage for the VCO to create an output frequency of 2.4MHz, it is in lock after
approximately 0.1ms, and a overshoot is also seen as expected, which in more detailed is explained in
section 5. It is again seen at 0.4ms when the division is change that the loop regulates again to create
the needed control voltage to create an output of the VCO of 4.8MHz, and again at 0.8ms when the
division is set to 4 again.

Table 26 and 27 shows the corner simulation result from the CPPLL, a note shall be made that an error
under veriﬁcation occur, the minimum temperature was accidentally set to -70 degrees. So the total
system might perform better than showed, detailed simulation results can see in section 12.1.2. In the
tables the extracted values for the diﬀerent division ratios is made when the CPPLL is in lock. It can be
seen that the CPPLL is able over corner to lock to the correct frequency and the jitter is within spec.
The current consumption for the low input reference worse case is approx 11.5 µA for all blocks and
for the high input reference clock the maximum current consumption is approx 19.4 µA so both within
spec. The period jitter is also within spec over corner. Table 28 shows the results from 65 monte carlo
simulations for a reference frequency of 600kHz for the CPPLL.

9.4
Behavioral model noise results
The systems performance to noise was simulated in the behavioural model developed in section 5. Typical
noise values from each block was extracted and implemented in the Verilog-A modules that emulates the
noise. Each noise source could manually be turned on to see how the system aﬀects the noise contribution
from the speciﬁc block, and the total output noise could be simulated by turning on all noise sources at
once. A ﬁctive reference noise was also made to see the eﬀect of reference noise on the system if it is as
predicted in section 4.7.1.

10
Future work
All speciﬁcations was meet expect the phase noise at 10kHz and 20kHz, seen from ﬁgure 94. Further
work is needed to bring this level down, and it can be seen that it is the VCO that dominates the phase
noise, so the focus should be on reducing the phase noise of the VCO. One shall note that the simulation
data used in the noise model, is from typical corner, so one has locate worse case noise results for the
VCO in the appendix, and simulations for the noise for the PFD and CP needs to be run over corners,
and the same for the lowpass ﬁlter, and then these results can be implement this into the noise model
This was not done due to time constrains.
The main focus for the noise should be looked at for the loop ﬁlter and the VCO, as it can be seen from
ﬁgure 93 that it is the loop ﬁlter, and especially the VCO that contributes with the highest noise.
A further notice is that noise from the supply haven’t been simulated or substrate, since it was assumed
that the supply voltage would be a well regulated, with very low noise. If this assumption was found out
wasn’t true, one has to verify the performance degraded on the system this will have. It is well known
that the VCO is very sensitive to supply and substrate noise [15, p. 188]. If supply and substrate noise is
of concern one also has to redesign the output buﬀer from the VCO which now is only an inverter stage,
this should be change out with for example a schmitt trigger so multiple false output clock signal from
the VCO won’t occur due to noisy output signal.
If it was found that the phase oﬀset that is in the CPPLL due to mismatch in current output of the CP
isn’t acceptable, one has to ﬁx the mismatch between the UP and DOWN signal from the PFD, with
the corporation with the CP output switches for the sink and source current, since these switches are
diﬀerent in size, and is made out of pmos and nmos transistors with diﬀerent threshold and will vary
diﬀerently over PVT. Next the output current of the CP should be increased, so it is able to react with a
more precise output current for small pulse width of the UP and DOWN signal. The increase in current
will help faster charge parasitic capacitance, and when the output current, is average over the pulse
width of either the UP or DOWN signal, the small loss in output current due to charging parasitic will
be much less compared to a smaller CP output current. Further one can increase the bias current for
the ampliﬁers to increase their slewrate, gain bandwidth and gain which will make them able to reach
and correct changes in the source or sink current faster.
One can safe current consumption in the system by reducing the way bias current is made. In many cases
one to one current mirroring is done, where it would be multiplied up from a lower reference current to
the desired bias current, one should however known this will reduce current matching. Especially the
CP current can be saved by lowering the bias current running in the resistors R1 and R3 in ﬁgure 46
and then scaling it up by the ratio between the resistors as seen in equations 112 and 113.
Since only a small amount of monte carlo simulations was done on top level, one should manually instead
change the values of the loop component to the estimated worse case as explain in section 5 where the
bandwidth of the CPPLL is so high compared to the low reference frequency that it might not work as
a LTI system any more and can be unstable.
If it is found the the capacitor of C1 is to large, two things can be done, one can increase the CP
current to keep the same loop bandwidth, however the resistor is needed to be increase, which will in-
crease thermal noise in the loop ﬁlter. Second option is to implement a mos capacitor instead of the
mim capacitor, however this will introduce much more leakage, which will increase spurs level as explain
in section 4.9. Further the phase oﬀset will also be increase if more leakage is introduces in the loop ﬁlter.
One thing that is needed to be check is the spurs level from the amount of worse case leakage current
and phase oﬀset, this was not done due to time constrains, however the leakage current from the CP is
simulated and presented so one has to implement the worse case results into equation 96, also the spurs
contribution from the current mismatch of CP needs to be calculated with equation 94.
Page 109 of 157
==PAGE:111==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
If the assumption of a clean reference clock doesn’t hold, one has to look a the compromise between noise
from reference and VCO and ﬁnd the optimal bandwidth of the PLL. One can use section 4.7.2 to help
ﬁnd a optimum between damping and loop bandwidth. One shall note that decreasing the bandwidth
of the PLL, thus more of the low frequency noise of the VCO will be observed, so even larger care to
reduce VCO noise is needed.
It was found from simulations that the source ampliﬁers has reduced gain in some corners and in monte
carlo, so an optimization of this ampliﬁer should be done.
Last monte carlo simulations is needed for the PFD and CP. This is due to failed MC runs, where if
they are run individually gives a correct result. So the problem why the run fails needs investigation
so full MC can be run, it is assumed that the system will still be functionally, since corner results show
functionality for the PFD and CP, and the ampliﬁers works in MC. It can be assumed extra phase oﬀset
will be observed for the MC simulations due to extra mismatch.
Page 110 of 157
==PAGE:112==
Ultra low power frequency multiplier
Emil Jakobsen
11
Conclusion
An investigation of how to create low power frequency synthesizers was made, and the choice for the
implementation was a CPPLL. System analysis was created to verify what system parameters that
eﬀect the CPPLL in what way, to help the designer create the desired system. Behavioural models of
system response and noise performance was created in cadence virtuoso to evaluate if calculated system
parameters will create the desired response and noise level. A CPPLL was then implemented in cadence
virtuoso out from the found speciﬁcations for each block, and simulated and veriﬁed. It is assumed that
the CPPLL will live up to all speciﬁcations expect phase noise, which is explained in section 10 what
could be done to improve this. Since it is a assumption that the CPPLL will live up to speciﬁcations is
that re simulation on monte carlo for the charge pump is needed, as explained in section 10.

Simulation result of functionality of VCO. Top plot show the internal voltage swing from

80
65
Simulation result for tuning and gain curve vs control voltage for typical corner. Simulation
results is created from a PSS simulations, with the setups shown in ﬁgure 95. It can be
seen from the top plot which shows tuning curve vs control voltage, that to create 2.4MHz
output frequency which is the lowest needed produced, a control voltage of approx 468mV
is needed, and for the higest needed frequency of the 24MHz a control voltage of approx
924mV is needed. The lower plot which shows the vco gain vs control voltage, which is
produces by diﬀerentiate the tuning curve, the gain over the frequency range has a gain
diﬀerence of approx 3.35.

12.3
Testbenches used for simulation
Figure 96: Schematic of the testbench setup for veriﬁcation of CP and PFD, to validate collaborated
performance in cadence Virtuose
The schematic shown in ﬁgure 96 shows the testbench to validate the collaboration of the PFD and CP
together to verify how large the mismatch between the current output current is. The bias current for
the CP is made with the circuit seen to the left, consisting of a a current mirror that mirrors a 1µA ,
into a transistor that has same size as M17 in ﬁgure 52, and the transistor below has same size as M1
in ﬁgure 52 so same VGS voltage is ensured. The circuit to the right of the CP creates the bias current
for the opamps inside the CP.
Figure 97: Schematic of the testbench setup for veriﬁcation of the whole CPPLL for transient simulation
in cadence Virtuose
The schematic shown in ﬁgure 97 shows the top level testbench for the CPPLL. The circuit to the left
and right creates the bias current for the CP and the opamps in the CP as explain above. Below each
block is a very small resistor 1nΩto measure the current consumption for each individual block. This
was done on top level since over PVT, biasing for each block would be diﬀerent from each other, due
to the dynamic behaviour between each block. The top level testbench was used to check if the system
could over PVT go into lock and produce the correct frequency, and to evaluate how large the jitter of
the system would be.

Figure 102: Schematic of the testbench setup for veriﬁcation of the source ampliﬁer in cadence Virtuose
The testbench shown in ﬁgure 102 shows the setup to verify the functionality of the ampliﬁer used for the
source current mirror in the CP. The testbench was created copies the setup from the CP with the top
resistors, the bottom left resistor, and bias circuit for the ampliﬁer and the biasing for the CP current
ﬂow. This was done to make sure that the ampliﬁer was in the right conditions as it would be in the
CP. The control voltage could be control to verify is the ampliﬁer was able to create the desired output
voltage so the voltage drop over the top resistors was constant. Further for stability analysis the feedback
loop created by the setup of the CP was needed.

13
Learning objectives, time plan and Self evaluation
13.1
Learning objectives
Learning objectives from the student handbook for Master student at DTU in the department of electrical
engineering
 Can identify and reﬂect on technical scientiﬁc issues and understand the interaction between the
various components that make up an issue.
 Can, on the basis of a clear academic proﬁle, apply elements of current research at international
level to develop ideas and solve problems.
 Masters technical scientiﬁc methodologies, theories and tools, and has the capacity to take a holistic
view of and delimit a complex, open issue, see it in a broader academic and societal perspective
and, on this basis, propose a variety of possible actions.
 Can, via analysis and modelling, develop relevant models, systems and processes for solving tech-
nological problems.
 Can communicate and mediate research-based knowledge both orally and in writing.
 Is familiar with and can seek out leading international research within his/her specialist area.
 Can work independently and reﬂect on own learning, academic development and specialization.
 Masters technical problem-solving at a high level through project work, and has the capacity to
work with and manage all phases of a project – including preparation of timetables, design, solution
and documentation.
Learning objectives created by the author
 Acquire knowledge in diﬀerent ways frequency synthesizers can be implemented.
 Acquire knowledge of system parameters, like phase noise, jitter, spurs, acquisition of lock for a
frequency synthesizer.
 Create a behavioural model of a frequency synthesizer that can help the designer to speciﬁed what
system parameters that eﬀect performance.
 Implement the found topologies for the frequency synthesizer in cadences Virtuose.
 Create a report that detailed explains how a frequency synthesizer works, and performs.
 Get further understanding of how Cadence Spectre RF simulator is used for evaluation of a fre-
quency synthesizer.

13.3
Self evaluation
During the project I found that designing of a PLL needed many of my skills I have achieved over the
period of both my bachelor and my master. Knowledge in system controlling, digital signal processing,
the z and Laplace transform, radio frequency theory, analog circuit theory and digital circuit theory to
mention some. I underestimated in the start of the project how much theory that needs to be covered to
fully understand all the diﬀerent system parameters and how to correctly simulate it in cadence virtuose.
Many times during implementation of diﬀerent blocks or behavioural models extra time was needed to
study literature to understand how they aﬀect the performance of the frequency synthesizer. Personally
I think I have put a great eﬀort to create a frequency synthesizer that lives up to all specs over PVT
and monte carlo expect the phase noise at 10k and 20kHz, however it is known how to reduce it as
describe in section 10. The report created for the project should give the idea how to create a frequency
synthesizer and what topologies that ﬁts for low power consumption. Further references to books and
papers is presented to the reader if more knowledges is desired. It has been a great challenge to create a
frequency synthesizer and I wish I had more time to dig into the theory, but overall I am satisﬁed with
the project.
